Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
Spread of Anthropomorphism by Converts
Ibn Asakir describes the infiltration of anthropomorphism by former scriptuaries and its spread.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how former Jews, Christians, and Magians introduced anthropomorphism into early Islamic thought and why the Umayyads failed to stop it.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphism in Islam was fueled by converts who disguised their former beliefs as hadith.

Ibn Asakir shows that the spread of tashbih was not accidental but partly due to insincere converts who mixed their old mythologies with Islamic narratives. The Umayyad dynasty's lack of doctrinal vigilance allowed these ideas to flourish, especially among uncritical traditionists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups that spread tashbih
The passage names Jewish rabbis, Christian monks, and Magian priests as the primary sources of anthropomorphic ideas.
These individuals professed Islam but secretly spread their previous beliefs.
2
Understand how they propagated
They targeted uncultured Bedouin narrators and naive clients, who accepted these myths as genuine Islamic reports.
Sometimes attributions to the Prophet were fabricated.
3
Note the Umayyad negligence
Banu Umayyah were not vigilant about doctrine except when it affected their politics. This allowed anthropomorphism to spread, especially among the Hushwiyya narrators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

) في كتابه ( رد أهل الأهواء والبدع وهم سموا أنفسهم معتزلة وذلك عندما بايع الحسن بن علی علیه السلام معاوية وسلم إليه الأمر اعتزلوا الحسن ومعاوية وجميع الناس وكانوا من أصحاب على ولزموا منازلهم ومساجدهم وقالوا نشتغل بالعلم والعبادة قسموا بذلك معتزلة هـ.
And the heretics among the Rawafid were a fertile pasture for planting their seeds, every time oppression of the Ahl al-Bayt by Banu Umayyah and others recurred. And when al-Hasan al-Sibt relinquished the caliphate to Mu'awiya, a group withdrew from both parties and remained in their mosques, occupying themselves with knowledge and worship, and before that they were wherever they were, and they are the origin of the Mu'tazila. (1) And it is said that the first to practice i'tizal were Abu Hashim 'Abdullah and al-Hasan, the two sons of Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah. Then the second began refuting the Khawarij on the issue of iman and he said: iman is the word and the contract without actions. So he and his group were called Murji'a because they delayed action from iman. And from them there arose a group that said: no sin harms with iman - they are the Murji'a of innovation. And there were many Jewish rabbis, Christian monks, and Magian priests who professed Islam during the time of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, and then after them they began to spread what they had of myths among those who were deceived from among the uncultured Bedouin narrators and their naive clients. So they seized them from them and narrated them to others with sound inner conviction, believing what was in their reports about Allah of anthropomorphism (tajsim) and comparison (tashbih), and finding familiar what they had of belief in their pre-Islamic ignorance. And sometimes they would falsely attribute them to the Prophet (PBUH) or by mistake. So tashbih began to seep into the belief of the sects and spread like a scandal. And Banu Umayyah were not like the Rightly Guided Caliphs in vigilance over the belief of Muslims, except in what touched their politics. The first to be deceived by them were the Shi'a, but soon they retreated from debating the Mu'tazila, and it did not persist among them as it did among the Hushwiyya of narrators. And Basra was the port of opinions and sects. There, Ma'bad ibn Khalid al-Juhani heard someone excusing sin by predestination, so he refuted him, denying that predestination negates choice in human actions, intending to defend the legitimacy of obligations. But his expression was inadequate, and he said: 'No predestination and the matter is new.' When that reached Ibn Umar, he disassociated from him. So Ma'bad's group were called Qadariyya, and his doctrine persisted among the common narrators of Basra for centuries. Rather, it evolved among a group of them to the extent that they attributed to the Creator what the Zoroastrians (?) attribute to light, and to the creature what they attribute to darkness. And Ghaylan ibn Muslim al-Dimashqi was spreading Ma'bad's view in Damascus, so Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz summoned him, forbade him, exposed his fallacy, so he desisted and said: 'O Commander of the Faithful...' (1) Abu al-Husayn al-Tara'ifi al-Shafi'i (d. 377 AH) said in his book 'Rad Ahl al-Ahwa' wa al-Bida': 'They named themselves Mu'tazila because when al-Hasan ibn Ali (peace be upon him) pledged allegiance to Mu'awiya and handed over the command, they withdrew from al-Hasan and Mu'awiya and all the people, and they were among the companions of Ali, and they remained in their houses and mosques, saying: we occupy ourselves with knowledge and worship. They were called Mu'tazila by that.'
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage combines several doctrinal narratives. It begins by stating that the heretical Rawafid exploited oppression of Ahl al-Bayt. Then it explains the origin of Mu'tazila: a group that withdrew (i'tizal) after Hasan's abdication, occupying themselves with worship. Then it mentions the Murji'a arising from al-Hasan ibn Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah, who defined iman as word and contract without actions, and the extreme Murji'a who said sin does not harm with iman. Next, it discusses the spread of tashbih (anthropomorphism) by former Jews, Christians, and Magians who pretended Islam and spread myths, leading to a widespread problem. Banu Umayyah are criticized for not safeguarding doctrine. Then it describes the rise of Qadariyya: Ma'bad al-Juhani's denial of predetermination negating choice, his phrase 'no predestination and the matter is new,' leading to the label Qadariyya. The doctrine evolved among some to dualistic tendencies. Finally, Ghaylan spread this in Damascus but was corrected by Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz. The footnote by al-Tara'ifi provides an alternative account of the naming of Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
تجسيم
تشبيه
حشوية
بنو أمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anthropomorphism in Islam arose from internal Islamic scriptural interpretation.
Correction: Ibn Asakir attributes its origin largely to converts from other religions who disguised their prior beliefs as Islamic narratives.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, which groups primarily spread anthropomorphism?
A) The Mu'tazila
B) Jewish rabbis, Christian monks, and Magian priests
C) The Umayyad rulers
D) The Shi'a

Key Takeaway
Former scripture followers exploited their conversion to inject anthropomorphism into early Muslim tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a religious story or teaching without verifying its source? How can you protect yourself from being deceived like the naive narrators described in this lesson?
?
The Umayyads neglected correct doctrine unless it affected their politics. Are there areas of your faith where you prioritize worldly concerns over the truth of tawhid? What must you change today?
?
The converts found comfort in anthropomorphism because it matched their former beliefs. What false comforts from your past (in ignorance or other belief systems) might you still be tempted to cling to? Will you surrender them completely to Allah as He is, beyond your imagination?

Sects and Disagreements

6 min
The Qadariyya: Ma'bad, Ghaylan, and the Correction
Ibn Asakir recounts the origin of the Qadariyya and its correction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early Qadariyya movement through its founder Ma'bad al-Juhani and the official correction by Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya arose from an overcorrection against predestination, claiming humans create their own actions.

Ibn Asakir now turns to the Qadariyya, a sect that emerged in Basra when Ma'bad al-Juhani tried to defend human responsibility but went too far, denying divine predestination entirely. This view later evolved into extreme dualism, and was officially refuted by the caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz.

Guided Path
1
Read the account of Ma'bad
Understand the context: someone excused sin by predestination. Ma'bad responded by denying that predestination negates human choice, but his statement was extreme.
His phrase 'لا قدر والأمر أنف' means 'No predestination; the matter is new.'
2
Learn about the reaction and label
Ibn Umar disassociated from him, and his followers were called Qadariyya.
3
Follow the later development
The doctrine evolved into dualism. Ghaylan spread it in Damascus; Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz corrected him.
Umar's intervention shows early caliphal authority in doctrinal matters.

المخلوق ما يعزونه إلى الظلمة، وكان غيلان بن مسلم الدمشقي ينشر بدمشق رأی معبد فطلبه عمر بن عبد العزيز ونهاه عن ذلك وكشف شبهته فانتهى وقال ( يا أمير (١) قال أبو الحسين الطرائفى الشافعي (المتوفى سنة (۳۷۷ Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadariyya believed in complete determinism.
Correction: They actually affirmed human choice so strongly that they denied any divine predestination, as seen in Ma'bad's statement 'No predestination; the matter is new.'

Checkpoint
What was Ma'bad al-Juhani's controversial statement?
A) Everything is by destiny
B) No predestination and the matter is new
C) Actions are from Allah alone
D) Iman is only in the heart

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya emerged from an extreme denial of divine predestination, triggering official correction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ma'bad intended to defend Allah's commands but ended up denying His decree. Have you ever spoken about Allah without knowledge, thinking you were defending the truth? What will you do this week to ensure your words about religion are based on sound learning, not just good intentions?
?
The Qadariyya's extreme view evolved into a dualistic belief that evil comes from a source other than Allah. In your heart, when trials come, do you sometimes attribute the difficulty to ‘bad luck’ or ‘circumstances’ rather than affirming that everything is from Allah? Will you consciously remind yourself, ‘I believe in Qadr, its good and its bad from Allah’?
?
When Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz corrected Ghaylan, he desisted. If a scholar with authority corrected a belief you hold, would you submit immediately like Ghaylan or cling to your view? What would it take for you to say, ‘I repent to Allah’ and change? Ponder on your attachment to your own opinions.

Origin of the Mu'tazila and the Early Murji'a
Ibn 'Asakir explains the origin of the Mu'tazila and the emergence of the Murji'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early origins of the Mu'tazila and how the Murji'a sect emerged from debates on the definition of faith.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila and Murji'a both began as responses to political and theological conflicts.

This lesson explores the early roots of two influential sects: the Mu'tazila and the Murji'a. Ibn 'Asakir traces their origin to the sons of Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya and their debates with the Khawarij. Understanding this background helps clarify later theological developments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Ibn 'Asakir connects the origin of the Mu'tazila to specific individuals.
The passage uses the term 'i'tizal' meaning withdrawal.
2
Identify the two groups mentioned
Identify the Mu'tazila and the Murji'a. Note that the Murji'a are further divided into two subgroups: those who say iman is word and conviction without works, and those who claim no sin harms with iman.
3
Reflect on the theological issue
Consider why the definition of iman was such a contentious issue. How does reducing iman to inner belief affect the role of works?
This debate would shape later Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على حيثما كان وهم أصل المعتزلة (١) ويقال أن أول من قام بالاعتزال أبو هاشم عبد الله والحسن إبنا محمد بن الحنفية، ثم أخذ الثاني يرد على الخوارج في مسألة الإيمان ويقول الإيمان هو الكلمة والعقد دون الأعمال فسمى هو وجماعته مرجئة لتأخيرهم العمل عن الإيمان وحدث منهم طائفة تقول : لا يضر مع الإيمان معصية وهم مرجئة البدعة وكان عدة من احبار اليهود ورهبان النصارى وموابذة المجوس اظهروا الإسلام في عهد الراشدين ثم اخذوا بعدهم في بث ماعندهم من الأساطير بين من تروج عليهم ممن لم يتهذب بالعلم من أعراب الرواة وبسطاء مواليهم فتلقفوها منهم ورووها لآخرين بسلامة باطن معتقدين مافي أخبارهم في جانب الله، من التجسيم والتشبيه ومستانسين بما كانوا عليه من الاعتقاد في جاهليتهم، وقد يرفعونها افتراء إلى الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم أو خطأ فأخذ التشبيه يتسرب إلى معتقد الطوائف ويشيع شيوع الفاحشة، ولم يكن بنو أمية كالراشدين في السهر على معتقد المسلمين إلا فيما يمس بسياستهم فأول من انخدع بهم الشيعة ولكن سرعان ما تراجعوا عن بمناظرة المعتزلة لهم، ولم يدم فيهم دوامه بين حشوية الرواة وكانت البصرة بندر الآراء والنحل، وقد سمع هناك معبد بن خالد الجهني من يتعلل في المعصية بالقدر فقام بالرد عليه ينفى كون القدر سالبا للاختيار في أفعال العباد، وهو يريد الدفاع عن شرعية التكاليف فضاقت عبارته وقال ( لاقدر والأمر أنف ) .
قال أبو الحسين الطرائفى الشافعي (المتوفى سنة (۳۷۷ ) في كتابه ( رد أهل الأهواء والبدع وهم سموا أنفسهم معتزلة وذلك عندما بايع الحسن بن علی علیه السلام معاوية وسلم إليه الأمر اعتزلوا الحسن ومعاوية وجميع الناس وكانوا من أصحاب على ولزموا منازلهم ومساجدهم وقالوا نشتغل بالعلم والعبادة قسموا بذلك معتزلة هـ.
Wherever they were, the origin of the Mu'tazila (1) is said to be that the first to practice i'tizal were Abu Hashim 'Abd Allah and al-Hasan, the two sons of Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya. Then the second began to refute the Khawarij on the matter of iman and said that iman is the word and the conviction without works, so he and his group were called Murji'a for postponing works from iman. And from them arose a faction saying: no sin harms with iman, and they are the Murji'a of innovation. A number of Jewish rabbis, Christian monks, and Zoroastrian priests had professed Islam in the era of the Rightly-Guided Caliphs, then after them they began to spread the myths they had among those who were taken in by them, among those who had not been refined by knowledge, from the Bedouin narrators and their simple clients. They received them from them and transmitted them to others with sound hearts, believing what was in their reports concerning God of anthropomorphism and likening, finding familiarity with what they had believed in their pre-Islamic days, and sometimes they falsely attributed them to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) or errantly. So anthropomorphism began to seep into the creed of the sects and spread like a scandal. The Umayyads were not like the Rightly-Guided Caliphs in guarding the creed of the Muslims except in matters touching their politics. The first to be deceived by them were the Shi'a, but they soon retreated from debating the Mu'tazila, and it did not last among them as it lasted among the Hashwiyya narrators. Basra was the port of opinions and sects. There Ma'bad ibn Khalid al-Juhani heard someone excusing sin by predestination, so he rose to refute him, denying that predestination negates choice in the actions of servants, intending to defend the legitimacy of religious obligations. His expression became constrained and he said: 'There is no predestination and the matter is new.' 
Abu al-Husayn al-Tara'ifi al-Shafi'i (d. 377 AH) said in his book 'Refutation of the People of Desires and Innovations': 'They called themselves Mu'tazila because when al-Hasan ibn 'Ali (peace be upon him) gave allegiance to Mu'awiya and ceded authority to him, they withdrew from al-Hasan, Mu'awiya, and all the people. They were among the followers of 'Ali and stayed in their homes and mosques, saying: we occupy ourselves with knowledge and worship, so they were called Mu'tazila.'
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative. Ibn 'Asakir begins by stating the origin of the name 'Mu'tazila' and notes that the first to practice i'tizal were Abu Hashim and al-Hasan, sons of Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya. The second son (al-Hasan) argued against the Khawarij on iman, saying iman is only word and conviction, not works, leading to the label 'Murji'a'. Some Murji'a went further, claiming no sin harms with iman. Then Ibn 'Asakir describes how converts from Judaism, Christianity, and Zoroastrianism introduced myths and anthropomorphism into Islamic tradition, which spread among unsophisticated narrators. The Umayyads were lax in guarding correct creed. The Shi'a were initially deceived but soon retreated. Basra became a hub of sects. There, Ma'bad al-Juhani heard someone using predestination to excuse sin and refuted him, denying that predestination negates free will, but his expression was inadequate, leading to his statement 'There is no predestination and the matter is new.' The footnote by al-Tara'ifi explains that the Mu'tazila were named for their withdrawal from political strife when al-Hasan ibn 'Ali gave allegiance to Mu'awiya.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
المرجئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Murji'a believed that works are completely irrelevant to faith.
Correction: The passage presents two Murji'a positions: one that iman excludes works but does not deny their importance, and another that claims sin does not harm, which is more extreme.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn 'Asakir, what did the second son (al-Hasan) say about iman?
A) Iman is word and conviction without works.
B) Iman includes all acts of obedience.
C) Iman increases and decreases with works.
D) Iman is only works.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and Murji'a both began as responses to political and theological issues, not as pure theological schools.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you genuinely feel that your faith is diminished, or do you comfort yourself by thinking that as long as you believe, it doesn't matter? How does this lesson challenge that comfort?
?
Have you ever withdrawn from a community or a responsibility because of political or personal conflict, like the early Mu'tazila? What would it take for you to stay and engage instead?
?
The early Murji'a defined faith without works, but an extremist faction took it further. Examine your own heart: do you ever separate your belief from your actions, assuming that your faith alone is sufficient? What concrete step will you take today to unite your faith with your deeds?

Infiltration of Anthropomorphic Beliefs
Ibn 'Asakir narrates how converts introduced anthropomorphic ideas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how converts from other religions introduced anthropomorphic ideas into early Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Foreign myths infiltrated Islamic tradition through recent converts and spread among the unsophisticated.

Ibn 'Asakir describes a critical phenomenon: converts from Judaism, Christianity, and Zoroastrianism brought their pre-Islamic myths into Islam, leading to anthropomorphic conceptions of God. This lesson examines that process and its impact on early Islamic sects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on infiltration
Read the Arabic and translation describing how converts spread myths. Pay attention to the terms used for the converts' backgrounds.
The passage lists Jewish rabbis, Christian monks, and Zoroastrian priests.
2
Identify the mechanism of spread
Notice that these converts transmitted their myths to Bedouin narrators and simple clients who accepted them uncritically. Then they passed them on to others.
3
Understand the doctrinal consequence
The result was the spreading of 'tajsim' (anthropomorphism) and 'tashbih' (likening God to creation). Consider how this affected early Islamic creed.
This background helps explain later polemics against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على حيثما كان وهم أصل المعتزلة (١) ويقال أن أول من قام بالاعتزال أبو هاشم عبد الله والحسن إبنا محمد بن الحنفية، ثم أخذ الثاني يرد على الخوارج في مسألة الإيمان ويقول الإيمان هو الكلمة والعقد دون الأعمال فسمى هو وجماعته مرجئة لتأخيرهم العمل عن الإيمان وحدث منهم طائفة تقول : لا يضر مع الإيمان معصية وهم مرجئة البدعة وكان عدة من احبار اليهود ورهبان النصارى وموابذة المجوس اظهروا الإسلام في عهد الراشدين ثم اخذوا بعدهم في بث ماعندهم من الأساطير بين من تروج عليهم ممن لم يتهذب بالعلم من أعراب الرواة وبسطاء مواليهم فتلقفوها منهم ورووها لآخرين بسلامة باطن معتقدين مافي أخبارهم في جانب الله، من التجسيم والتشبيه ومستانسين بما كانوا عليه من الاعتقاد في جاهليتهم، وقد يرفعونها افتراء إلى الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم أو خطأ فأخذ التشبيه يتسرب إلى معتقد الطوائف ويشيع شيوع الفاحشة، ولم يكن بنو أمية كالراشدين في السهر على معتقد المسلمين إلا فيما يمس بسياستهم فأول من انخدع بهم الشيعة ولكن سرعان ما تراجعوا عن بمناظرة المعتزلة لهم، ولم يدم فيهم دوامه بين حشوية الرواة وكانت البصرة بندر الآراء والنحل، وقد سمع هناك معبد بن خالد الجهني من يتعلل في المعصية بالقدر فقام بالرد عليه ينفى كون القدر سالبا للاختيار في أفعال العباد، وهو يريد الدفاع عن شرعية التكاليف فضاقت عبارته وقال ( لاقدر والأمر أنف ) .
قال أبو الحسين الطرائفى الشافعي (المتوفى سنة (۳۷۷ ) في كتابه ( رد أهل الأهواء والبدع وهم سموا أنفسهم معتزلة وذلك عندما بايع الحسن بن علی علیه السلام معاوية وسلم إليه الأمر اعتزلوا الحسن ومعاوية وجميع الناس وكانوا من أصحاب على ولزموا منازلهم ومساجدهم وقالوا نشتغل بالعلم والعبادة قسموا بذلك معتزلة هـ.
Wherever they were, the origin of the Mu'tazila (1) is said to be that the first to practice i'tizal were Abu Hashim 'Abd Allah and al-Hasan, the two sons of Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya. Then the second began to refute the Khawarij on the matter of iman and said that iman is the word and the conviction without works, so he and his group were called Murji'a for postponing works from iman. And from them arose a faction saying: no sin harms with iman, and they are the Murji'a of innovation. A number of Jewish rabbis, Christian monks, and Zoroastrian priests had professed Islam in the era of the Rightly-Guided Caliphs, then after them they began to spread the myths they had among those who were taken in by them, among those who had not been refined by knowledge, from the Bedouin narrators and their simple clients. They received them from them and transmitted them to others with sound hearts, believing what was in their reports concerning God of anthropomorphism and likening, finding familiarity with what they had believed in their pre-Islamic days, and sometimes they falsely attributed them to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) or errantly. So anthropomorphism began to seep into the creed of the sects and spread like a scandal. The Umayyads were not like the Rightly-Guided Caliphs in guarding the creed of the Muslims except in matters touching their politics. The first to be deceived by them were the Shi'a, but they soon retreated from debating the Mu'tazila, and it did not last among them as it lasted among the Hashwiyya narrators. Basra was the port of opinions and sects. There Ma'bad ibn Khalid al-Juhani heard someone excusing sin by predestination, so he rose to refute him, denying that predestination negates choice in the actions of servants, intending to defend the legitimacy of religious obligations. His expression became constrained and he said: 'There is no predestination and the matter is new.' 
Abu al-Husayn al-Tara'ifi al-Shafi'i (d. 377 AH) said in his book 'Refutation of the People of Desires and Innovations': 'They called themselves Mu'tazila because when al-Hasan ibn 'Ali (peace be upon him) gave allegiance to Mu'awiya and ceded authority to him, they withdrew from al-Hasan, Mu'awiya, and all the people. They were among the followers of 'Ali and stayed in their homes and mosques, saying: we occupy ourselves with knowledge and worship, so they were called Mu'tazila.'
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative. Ibn 'Asakir begins by stating the origin of the name 'Mu'tazila' and notes that the first to practice i'tizal were Abu Hashim and al-Hasan, sons of Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya. The second son (al-Hasan) argued against the Khawarij on iman, saying iman is only word and conviction, not works, leading to the label 'Murji'a'. Some Murji'a went further, claiming no sin harms with iman. Then Ibn 'Asakir describes how converts from Judaism, Christianity, and Zoroastrianism introduced myths and anthropomorphism into Islamic tradition, which spread among unsophisticated narrators. The Umayyads were lax in guarding correct creed. The Shi'a were initially deceived but soon retreated. Basra became a hub of sects. There, Ma'bad al-Juhani heard someone using predestination to excuse sin and refuted him, denying that predestination negates free will, but his expression was inadequate, leading to his statement 'There is no predestination and the matter is new.' The footnote by al-Tara'ifi explains that the Mu'tazila were named for their withdrawal from political strife when al-Hasan ibn 'Ali gave allegiance to Mu'awiya.

Key Terms
التجسيم
التشبيه
الحشوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anthropomorphism (tashbih) was an original Islamic belief.
Correction: Ibn 'Asakir traces it to converts from other religions who brought their pre-Islamic myths into Islamic tradition.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn 'Asakir, who was responsible for spreading anthropomorphic ideas in early Islam?
A) The Rightly-Guided Caliphs
B) Converts from Judaism, Christianity, and Zoroastrianism
C) The Mu'tazila
D) The Khawarij

Key Takeaway
Early Islamic theology was influenced by the backgrounds of converts, leading to the spread of anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah that felt comfortable because it matched something from your upbringing or culture, without checking if it aligns with the Quran and authentic Sunnah? What concrete step will you take today to verify or correct it?
?
This passage shows that even with sincere intentions, people can spread harmful ideas about Allah. Is there a teaching about Allah you have accepted without question from a popular speaker or relative? Will you examine it against the sources?
?
The converts in the story were influenced by their pre-Islamic beliefs. What non-Islamic ideas about God might still linger in your heart due to social or familial influences? How will you actively purify your understanding of tawhid from them?

Ma'bad al-Juhani and the First Qadariyya Statement
Ibn 'Asakir records the first Qadariyya statement and a footnote on Mu'tazila naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Qadariyya statement and its context in Basra, as well as the political origin of the Mu'tazila name.

Core Idea
Ma'bad al-Juhani's response to predestinarian excuses led to the Qadariyya slogan 'No predestination, the matter is new.'

This lesson focuses on the emergence of the Qadariyya in Basra. Ibn 'Asakir tells the story of Ma'bad al-Juhani refuting those who excused sin by predestination, and the footnote explains the political origin of the Mu'tazila name, linking it to the withdrawal after al-Hasan's peace with Mu'awiya.

Guided Path
1
Read the account of Ma'bad
Read the Arabic and translation describing the situation in Basra and Ma'bad's response. Note his intention to defend religious obligations.
His statement 'There is no predestination and the matter is new' became a slogan.
2
Understand the context
Notice that the Umayyads neglected creed except when politics was involved. The Shi'a were initially deceived but retreated. The Hashwiyya narrators persisted.
3
Examine the footnote
Read the footnote from al-Tara'ifi that provides an alternative origin for the name Mu'tazila, based on political withdrawal after al-Hasan's allegiance to Mu'awiya.
This shows the name had multiple historical explanations.
4
Connect the two narratives
Reflect on how the theological and political origins of the Mu'tazila might be related. Both involve withdrawal from conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
القدرية
معبد الجهني
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qadariyya completely denied that God has any control over human actions.
Correction: Ma'bad's intention was to defend free will for moral responsibility, not to deny divine power entirely. His slogan was a reaction to a extreme predestinarian excuse.

Checkpoint
According to the footnote, why were the Mu'tazila called by that name?
A) Because they withdrew from theological debate
B) Because they withdrew from political conflict after al-Hasan's peace treaty
C) Because they opposed free will
D) Because they rejected hadith

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya arose from a pastoral concern to preserve moral responsibility, and the Mu'tazila name had a political origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself blaming circumstances or fate? How does Ma'bad's concern for the 'legitimacy of religious obligations' challenge that habit, and what would you need to do differently this week?
?
Ma'bad's words came out constrained—he meant well but his phrase was taken as denial of predestination. Have you ever spoken a half-truth or oversimplified a belief, only to have it misrepresent your heart? What step can you take to ensure your words about faith are as accurate as your intentions?
?
The Shi'a retreated from debating; the Hashwiyya persisted. In which areas of your faith have you stopped seeking truth because of difficulty? Will you commit to finding a trusted source and re-engaging that topic this month?

The Origin of the Hashwiyya Sect
Ibn 'Asakir narrates origin of Hashwiyya from al-Hasan's circle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term Hashwiyya originated from Hasan al-Basri's dismissal of ignorant narrators and its association with anthropomorphist groups.

Core Idea
A dismissive remark gave rise to a sect name that stuck.

This passage introduces us to the scholarly circle of Hasan al-Basri, one of the great Successors. His interaction with certain ignorant narrators led to a term that would later label a whole group. Understanding this origin helps clarify early sectarian labels.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part of the passage
Focus on the description of Hasan al-Basri and his circle. Notice the status of his attendees and the incident with ignorant narrators.
The term 'hasha' means side or margin.
2
Identify the naming of Hashwiyya
Al-Hasan ordered the ignorant narrators to be moved to the side (hasha) of the circle. This led to them being called Hashwiyya. Note the connection with Mujassima and Mushabbiha.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider how a simple classroom management action became a label for a sect. This shows how labels can originate from specific incidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان الحسن البصرى من جلة التابعين وممن استمر سنين ينشر العلم في البصرة، ويلازم مجلسه نبلاء أهل العلم، وقد حضر مجلسه يوما أناس من رفاع الرواة ولما تكلموا بالسقط عنده قال ردوا هؤلاء إلى حشا الحلقة أى جانبها فسموا الحشوية ، ومنهم أصناف المجسمة والمشبهة، وكان واصل بن عطاء بعد أن أخذ الاعتزال عن أبي هاشم السابق ذكره يحضر في مجلس الحسن ، وقد ذكرت مسألة الإيمان فى المجلس فبادر واصل إلى القول بأن الكافر المجاهر والمؤمن المطيع لا خلاف في تسميتهما كافرا ومؤمنا، ومرتكب الكبيرة حيث كان موضع اختلاف في اطلاق احدهما عليه نابى اطلاق هذا وذاك عليه ونقول فيه انه فاسق أخذا بما اتفقوا وهجرا لما اختلفوا، وكأنه يريد التوسط بين الخلافين واستمالة الفريقين إلى رأيه، لكنه في المعنى مع الخوارج لأنه يرى الخلود في النار لمرتكب الكبيرة فلم يرتض الحسن كلامه، فانسحب واصل من المجلس وأخذ ينشر مذهب الاعتزال والأصول الخمسة
Al-Hasan al-Basri was among the eminent Successors and among those who continued for years spreading knowledge in Basra, and the nobles of the people of knowledge used to attend his session. One day, people from among the ignorant narrators attended his session, and when they spoke with errors, he said: 'Send these back to the side (hasha) of the circle'—i.e., its side—so they were called the Hashwiyya, and among them are the varieties of the Mujassima and Mushabbiha. Wasil ibn 'Ata', after he had received the Mu'tazila doctrine from Abu Hashim mentioned earlier, used to attend al-Hasan's session. The matter of iman was raised in the session, and Wasil hastened to say: 'The open disbeliever and the obedient believer are not disputed in their naming as disbeliever and believer. But the committer of a grave sin—since it is a point of disagreement in applying either term to him—I avoid applying this or that to him and say that he is a fasiq, taking what they agreed upon and abandoning what they disagreed on.' It was as if he wanted to mediate between the two differences and win over both factions to his view, but in essence he is with the Khawarij because he holds that the committer of a grave sin will abide eternally in the Fire. Al-Hasan did not approve of his words, so Wasil withdrew from the session and began to spread the Mu'tazila doctrine and the five principles.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage describes al-Hasan al-Basri's scholarly circle. Ignorant narrators spoke erroneously, so al-Hasan banished them to the side of the circle, giving rise to the name 'Hashwiyya', which includes anthropomorphists (Mujassima and Mushabbiha). Wasil ibn 'Ata', who had already adopted Mu'tazila teachings from Abu Hashim, attended al-Hasan's session. When the issue of iman was discussed, Wasil proposed a middle ground: the grave sinner is neither believer nor disbeliever but a fasiq. However, the author notes that this is essentially the Kharijite position since Wasil believed in eternal punishment for the grave sinner. Al-Hasan rejected this, and Wasil withdrew, thereafter spreading the Mu'tazila school and its five principles.

Key Terms
الحسن البصري
الحشوية
المجسمة
المشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why were the ignorant narrators called 'Hashwiyya'?
A) They were from the village of Hasha
B) Al-Hasan sent them to the side (hasha) of the circle
C) They were known for their hashish use
D) They were followers of a man named Hashwa

Key Takeaway
The term Hashwiyya originated from al-Hasan al-Basri's dismissal of ignorant narrators to the side of his circle, later associated with anthropomorphist sects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone in your circle of learning speaks with ignorance, how do you respond? Do you ever dismiss them in a way that could mark them permanently? Examine your own speech and its potential to create lasting divisions.
?
This story shows how a single offhand remark by al-Hasan al-Basri gave rise to a sect name that persisted for centuries. Are there any labels or nicknames you have given to fellow Muslims that you need to repent from? What will you do to repair the harm?
?
If you were among the ignorant narrators sent to the side, how would you have felt? Would you have returned humbly or nursed resentment? This lesson calls you to examine your heart when you are corrected—do you accept correction or do you allow pride to turn a moment of truth into a lifelong label?

Wasil ibn 'Ata and the Secession from Hasan al-Basri
Ibn 'Asakir details Wasil's proposal and secession from al-Hasan's circle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Wasil ibn 'Ata's proposed classification of the grave sinner as a fasiq led to his rejection by al-Hasan and the formation of the Mu'tazila school.

Core Idea
A compromise position on grave sinners that was rejected as too extreme.

The passage continues with Wasil ibn 'Ata, who had already studied Mu'tazila thought. During a discussion on faith (iman), he proposed a 'middle' view that the grave sinner is neither believer nor disbeliever but a fasiq. However, this view aligned with the Khawarij on eternal punishment, leading al-Hasan to reject it and causing Wasil to leave and establish his own school.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on Wasil ibn 'Ata's background and his attendance at al-Hasan's session. Notice how he had already taken the Mu'tazila doctrine from Abu Hashim.
Abu Hashim was a prominent early Mu'tazili.
2
Analyze Wasil's proposal
When the topic of iman came up, Wasil suggested that the grave sinner is a fasiq—avoiding both 'believer' and 'disbeliever'. Understand why he saw this as a middle ground.
3
Understand al-Hasan's reaction
Al-Hasan rejected the view because it effectively agreed with the Khawarij who hold that grave sinners will abide eternally in Hell. Consider why this made it unacceptable.
4
Note the outcome
Wasil withdrew from the session and began spreading the Mu'tazila doctrine and its five principles.

Source Text

وكان الحسن البصرى من جلة التابعين وممن استمر سنين ينشر العلم في البصرة، ويلازم مجلسه نبلاء أهل العلم، وقد حضر مجلسه يوما أناس من رفاع الرواة ولما تكلموا بالسقط عنده قال ردوا هؤلاء إلى حشا الحلقة أى جانبها فسموا الحشوية ، ومنهم أصناف المجسمة والمشبهة، وكان واصل بن عطاء بعد أن أخذ الاعتزال عن أبي هاشم السابق ذكره يحضر في مجلس الحسن ، وقد ذكرت مسألة الإيمان فى المجلس فبادر واصل إلى القول بأن الكافر المجاهر والمؤمن المطيع لا خلاف في تسميتهما كافرا ومؤمنا، ومرتكب الكبيرة حيث كان موضع اختلاف في اطلاق احدهما عليه نابى اطلاق هذا وذاك عليه ونقول فيه انه فاسق أخذا بما اتفقوا وهجرا لما اختلفوا، وكأنه يريد التوسط بين الخلافين واستمالة الفريقين إلى رأيه، لكنه في المعنى مع الخوارج لأنه يرى الخلود في النار لمرتكب الكبيرة فلم يرتض الحسن كلامه، فانسحب واصل من المجلس وأخذ ينشر مذهب الاعتزال والأصول الخمسة
Al-Hasan al-Basri was among the eminent Successors and among those who continued for years spreading knowledge in Basra, and the nobles of the people of knowledge used to attend his session. One day, people from among the ignorant narrators attended his session, and when they spoke with errors, he said: 'Send these back to the side (hasha) of the circle'—i.e., its side—so they were called the Hashwiyya, and among them are the varieties of the Mujassima and Mushabbiha. Wasil ibn 'Ata', after he had received the Mu'tazila doctrine from Abu Hashim mentioned earlier, used to attend al-Hasan's session. The matter of iman was raised in the session, and Wasil hastened to say: 'The open disbeliever and the obedient believer are not disputed in their naming as disbeliever and believer. But the committer of a grave sin—since it is a point of disagreement in applying either term to him—I avoid applying this or that to him and say that he is a fasiq, taking what they agreed upon and abandoning what they disagreed on.' It was as if he wanted to mediate between the two differences and win over both factions to his view, but in essence he is with the Khawarij because he holds that the committer of a grave sin will abide eternally in the Fire. Al-Hasan did not approve of his words, so Wasil withdrew from the session and began to spread the Mu'tazila doctrine and the five principles.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage describes al-Hasan al-Basri's scholarly circle. Ignorant narrators spoke erroneously, so al-Hasan banished them to the side of the circle, giving rise to the name 'Hashwiyya', which includes anthropomorphists (Mujassima and Mushabbiha). Wasil ibn 'Ata', who had already adopted Mu'tazila teachings from Abu Hashim, attended al-Hasan's session. When the issue of iman was discussed, Wasil proposed a middle ground: the grave sinner is neither believer nor disbeliever but a fasiq. However, the author notes that this is essentially the Kharijite position since Wasil believed in eternal punishment for the grave sinner. Al-Hasan rejected this, and Wasil withdrew, thereafter spreading the Mu'tazila school and its five principles.

Key Terms
واصل بن عطاء
الأصول الخمسة
فاسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wasil's position was a genuine middle ground between Khawarij and Murji'ah.
Correction: The author argues that Wasil's view is essentially Khawarij because he believes in eternal punishment for grave sinners, despite the nomenclature.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Hasan al-Basri reject Wasil ibn 'Ata's view on the grave sinner?
A) Wasil used wrong terminology
B) Wasil's view implied eternal punishment for grave sinners, aligning with the Khawarij
C) Al-Hasan believed grave sinners are believers
D) Wasil insulted al-Hasan

Key Takeaway
Wasil ibn 'Ata's attempt to define the grave sinner as a fasiq was rejected by al-Hasan because it implicitly adopted the Kharijite view of eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a clear division between truth and falsehood, do you ever try to 'mediate' in a way that actually blurs the line, like Wasil did? How can you ensure your desire for harmony does not lead you to compromise a core truth? What specific step will you take this week to avoid a false middle ground?
?
Wasil claimed to take what is agreed upon and abandon what is disputed—a seemingly wise approach. But the lesson shows it was a cover for an erroneous belief. Where in your own life do you use similar reasoning to evade uncomfortable truths from the Quran and Sunnah? Will you honestly examine one area where you have been 'mediating' between divine command and personal desire?
?
Al-Hasan al-Basri rejected Wasil's words even though they appeared moderate. Do you have enough spiritual discernment to recognize when a position that sounds balanced actually contradicts fundamental beliefs? Ask yourself: what is one popular 'balanced' opinion in your community that you have accepted without testing against the sources? Are you willing to stand against it if it undermines the truth?

Chain of Early Mu'tazila Transmitters
Ibn 'Asakir lists early Mu'tazila authorities and their chains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early transmission chain of the Mu'tazila, including the two main branches in Basra and Baghdad.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila spread through a chain of scholars from Wasil's companions.

Ibn 'Asakir provides a concise genealogy of early Mu'tazila leaders, showing how the school branched from Wasil ibn 'Ata' through his immediate students. This passage helps you trace the intellectual lineage and correct a common misconception about who studied with whom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing, then read the translation to grasp the overall chain.
Notice the two branches: one from Abu al-Hudhayl (Basra) and one from Bishr al-Mu'tamir (Baghdad).
2
Trace the Basra branch
Identify the line from Wasil to 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and Bishr ibn Sa'id, then to Abu al-Hudhayl, then to al-Nazzam, al-Jahiz, etc.
Note the correction about Ibn Abi Du'ad not meeting Wasil.
3
Trace the Baghdad branch
Follow the line from Wasil's companion Bishr ibn Sa'id to Bishr al-Mu'tamir, then to Abu Musa, then to the two Ja'fars, then to al-Askafi.
4
Identify later figures
Notice al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim from al-Shahham, and 'Abbad ibn Sulayman from al-Fuwati. These are later leaders.
This completes the list of 'leaders of the Mu'tazila in Basra and Baghdad.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع صاحبيه عمرو بن عبيد وبشر بن سعيد وعنهما أخذ بشر بن المعتمر وأبو الهذيل وبالثاني تخرج أبو بكر عبدالرحمن بن كيسان الأصم وإبراهيم النظام وهشام الفوطى وعلى بن محمد الشحام وعن النظام أخذ الجاحظ وابن أبي دؤاد - ولم يدرك واصلا كما ظن - وعن الأول انتشر الاعتزال ببغداد حيث اخذ منه أبو موسى بن صبيح وعنه جعفر بن حرب وجعفر بن مبشر وعنهما محمد بن عبد الله الاسكافى وعن الشحام أخذ الجبائي وعنه ابنه أبو هاشم، وأخذ عن الفوطى عباد بن سليمان فهؤلاء هم قادة الاعتزال في البصرة وبغداد
With his two companions 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and Bishr ibn Sa'id. From the two of them Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir and Abu al-Hudhayl learned. From the second graduated Abu Bakr 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Kaysan al-Asamm, Ibrahim al-Nazzam, Hisham al-Fuwati, and 'Ali ibn Muhammad al-Shahham. From al-Nazzam al-Jahiz and Ibn Abi Du'ad learned—and he did not reach Wasil as some thought. From the first the Mu'tazila spread in Baghdad, for from him Abu Musa ibn Subayh learned, and from him Ja'far ibn Harb and Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, and from the two of them Muhammad ibn 'Abd Allah al-Askafi. From al-Shahham al-Jubba'i learned, and from him his son Abu Hashim. From al-Fuwati, 'Abbad ibn Sulayman learned. These are the leaders of the Mu'tazila in Basra and Baghdad.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a list of the early Mu'tazila chain of transmission. It begins with Wasil's two companions, 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and Bishr ibn Sa'id. From them, Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir and Abu al-Hudhayl learned. From the second (Abu al-Hudhayl), several figures graduated: Abu Bakr al-Asamm, Ibrahim al-Nazzam, Hisham al-Fuwati, and 'Ali al-Shahham. From al-Nazzam, the famous al-Jahiz and Ibn Abi Du'ad studied. The text notes that Ibn Abi Du'ad did not actually meet Wasil, contrary to assumption. From the first (Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir), Mu'tazila spread in Baghdad: Abu Musa ibn Subayh learned from him, and from Abu Musa came Ja'far ibn Harb and Ja'far ibn Mubashshir, and from them Muhammad al-Askafi. From al-Shahham, al-Jubba'i learned, and from al-Jubba'i his son Abu Hashim. From al-Fuwati, 'Abbad ibn Sulayman learned. These are identified as the leaders of the Mu'tazila in Basra and Baghdad.

?

The Origins of the Jabriyya and Hashwiyya Sects
Ibn Asakir narrates the emergence of the Jabriyya and Hashwiyya sects from al-Hasan al-Basri's circle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two early Islamic sects, the Jabriyya and Hashwiyya, originated from specific historical incidents involving Jahm ibn Safwan and al-Hasan al-Basri.

Core Idea
Sectarian labels often arise from specific historical conflicts, not just doctrinal differences.

In this passage, Ibn Asakir traces the origins of two infamous sects: the Jabriyya, who overemphasized divine compulsion, and the Hashwiyya, a term for those who relied on hadith uncritically. He connects both to real events and people, showing how theological labels emerged from concrete situations. Let's read his account.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two stories
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that it contains two separate narratives: one about the Jabriyya and one about the Hashwiyya.
The passage begins with the Jabriyya and then moves to the Hashwiyya.
2
Identify the key persons
Who are Ma'bad, Jahm ibn Safwan, and al-Hasan al-Basri? What role does each play in the origin of these sects?
Ma'bad's opinion sparked a reaction; Jahm went to the opposite extreme; al-Hasan was a respected scholar whose classroom incident coined a term.
3
Reflect on the social dimension
How did a simple classroom instruction lead to a sectarian label? Consider how social dynamics and authority figures shape theological identities.
The term 'Hashwiyya' originally meant 'those at the back', not a doctrinal stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما بدأ يذيع رأى معبد أخذ في الرد عليه جهم بن صفوان بخراسان فوقع في الجبر ونشأ عنه مذهب الجبرية، وكان الحسن البصرى من جلة التابعين وممن استمر سنين ينشر العلم في البصرة، ويلازم مجلسه نبلاء أهل العلم، وقد حضر مجلسه يوما أناس من رفاع الرواة ولما تكلموا بالسقط عنده قال ردوا هؤلاء إلى حشا الحلقة أى جانبها فسموا الحشوية ، ومنهم أصناف المجسمة والمشبهة
And when the opinion of Ma'bad began to spread, Jahm ibn Safwan in Khurasan began to refute it, so he fell into jabr (compulsion) and from it arose the school of the Jabriyya. Al-Hasan al-Basri was among the eminent Successors and among those who continued for years spreading knowledge in Basra, and the nobles of the people of knowledge attended his circle. One day, some people from the Rifā' narrators attended his circle, and when they spoke with error in his presence, he said, 'Return these to the back of the circle,' i.e., its side, so they were called the Hashwiyya. Among them are the kinds of the Mujassima and Mushabbiha.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative that traces the origin of two early Islamic sects: the Jabriyya (those who assert compulsion) and the Hashwiyya (a pejorative term for those who rely on hadith without reason). Ibn Asakir begins by noting that when Ma'bad's opinion (likely about free will) began to spread, Jahm ibn Safwan refuted it but went to the opposite extreme, falling into jabr, giving rise to the Jabriyya. Then he turns to al-Hasan al-Basri's circle, where some narrators spoke incorrectly, prompting al-Hasan to order them to the back of the circle. From this incident, the term Hashwiyya was coined. The passage then explains that among the Hashwiyya are those who hold anthropomorphic views (Mujassima and Mushabbiha). This shows how sectarian labels originated from specific historical events.

Key Terms
جبرية
حشوية
مجسمة
مشبهة
الحسن البصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Jabriyya and Hashwiyya formed purely from theological differences without concrete historical events.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows they arose from specific reactions to individuals (Jahm's overcorrection of Ma'bad) and social dynamics (al-Hasan's classroom discipline).

Checkpoint

A) They were companions of Jahm.
B) Al-Hasan al-Basri ordered them to the back of the circle.
C) They supported Ma'bad's opinion.
D) They were from Khurasan.

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya arose from Jahm's overcorrection of Ma'bad's view, while the Hashwiyya got their name from al-Hasan al-Basri's dismissal of careless narrators to the back of the circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that sectarian labels often started from specific incidents, not just ideas. Think of a time you labeled someone (or a group) based on a single interaction. How might that label have been unfair or incomplete? What would it take for you to go back and reconsider that label?
?
Al-Hasan al-Basri dismissed those who spoke with error to the back of the circle, which gave them a lasting name. Are there people in your life whom you have 'sent to the back' — dismissed or marginalized — because of their mistakes? How might that affect their spiritual journey? What can you do today to invite them back?
?
Jahm ibn Safwan overcorrected Ma'bad's view and fell into the opposite extreme. When you hear an opinion you strongly disagree with, do you sometimes overreact and swing to another extreme? Can you identify an area in your beliefs where you might be doing that right now? What would it look like to seek a balanced, middle path?

Wasil ibn Ata's Departure and the Mu'tazila Founding
Ibn Asakir describes Wasil's innovative position on grave sinners and his break from al-Hasan al-Basri.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical event that led to the founding of the Mu'tazila school, focusing on Wasil's innovative position on the grave sinner and his break from al-Hasan al-Basri.

Core Idea
Wasil's middle position on grave sinners created a new theological school.

This passage from Ibn Asakir recounts the pivotal moment when Wasil ibn Ata broke away from al-Hasan al-Basri over the status of grave sinners. His innovative use of the term 'fasiq' and his agreement with the Khawarij on eternal punishment set the stage for the Mu'tazila movement. Let's examine the details of this founding event.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow of the narrative: the issue of faith, Wasil's intervention, and the outcome.
The Arabic word 'نابی' may be unclear; it likely means 'distanced himself'.
2
Identify Wasil's threefold classification
Wasil distinguishes three categories: the open disbeliever, the obedient believer, and the grave sinner. He reserves the term 'fasiq' for the latter. Notice how he claims this is a middle ground.
The Khawarij called grave sinners disbelievers; the Murji'ah called them believers. Wasil's term 'fasiq' is meant to avoid both extremes.
3
Consider the rejection and its consequences
The passage says al-Hasan did not approve of Wasil's words. As a result, Wasil withdrew from the circle and began to propagate Mu'tazilism. This marks the formal beginning of the Mu'tazila school.
The 'five principles' (al-usul al-khamsa) are mentioned here, though only the first (the status of grave sinners) is discussed directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان واصل بن عطاء بعد أن أخذ الاعتزال عن أبي هاشم السابق ذكره يحضر في مجلس الحسن ، وقد ذكرت مسألة الإيمان فى المجلس فبادر واصل إلى القول بأن الكافر المجاهر والمؤمن المطيع لا خلاف في تسميتهما كافرا ومؤمنا، ومرتكب الكبيرة حيث كان موضع اختلاف في اطلاق احدهما عليه نابی اطلاق هذا وذاك عليه ونقول فيه انه فاسق أخذا بما اتفقوا وهجرا لما اختلفوا، وكأنه يريد التوسط بين الخلافين واستمالة الفريقين إلى رأيه، لكنه في المعنى مع الخوارج لأنه يرى الخلود في النار لمرتكب الكبيرة فلم يرتض الحسن كلامه، فانسحب واصل من المجلس وأخذ ينشر مذهب الاعتزال والأصول الخمسة . مع صاحبيه عمرو بن عبيد وبشر بن سعيد
And Wasil ibn 'Ata, after taking i'tizal (withdrawal) from Abu Hashim mentioned earlier, used to attend al-Hasan's circle. The issue of faith was brought up in the circle, so Wasil hastened to say that the open disbeliever and the obedient believer are not disputed in calling them disbeliever and believer, but the committer of a major sin where there is disagreement in applying either term to him—he distanced himself from applying this or that to him and says about him that he is a fasiq (transgressor), taking what they agreed on and abandoning what they differed on. It is as if he wanted to mediate between the two opposing views and win over both groups to his opinion, but in meaning he is with the Khawarij because he holds that the committer of a major sin will abide forever in Hellfire. Al-Hasan did not approve of his words, so Wasil withdrew from the circle and began to spread the doctrine of i'tizal and the five principles, along with his two companions 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd and Bishr ibn Sa'id.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a historical account of Wasil ibn 'Ata's break from al-Hasan al-Basri, which marks the origin of the Mu'tazila school. The key issue is the status of the grave sinner (murtakib al-kabira). Wasil proposed a middle ground: the sinner is neither a believer nor a disbeliever but a fasiq. However, he still held that such a person would abide eternally in Hell, aligning him with the Khawarij on punishment. Al-Hasan rejected this, leading to Wasil's departure. The passage highlights the 'five principles' (al-usul al-khamsa) of the Mu'tazila, though only the first is implied here. The names of his companions indicate the early spread of the school.

Key Terms

مرتكب الكبيرة
الإيمان
فاسق
الخلود في النار
الأصول الخمسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila position on grave sinners was purely a moderate compromise without harsh consequences.
Correction: In fact, the passage states that Wasil 'in meaning is with the Khawarij because he holds that the committer of a major sin will abide forever in Hellfire.' Thus, while using a different label (fasiq instead of kafir), the Mu'tazila agreed with the Khawarij on eternal punishment.

Checkpoint

A) Believer
B) Disbeliever
C) Fasiq
D) Munafiq

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila school was founded when Wasil ibn 'Ata broke from al-Hasan al-Basri by labeling grave sinners as fasiqs while still affirming their eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own sins, do you find yourself more inclined to label yourself as a believer despite disobedience, or do you lean towards despair? How does Wasil’s avoidance of both extremes challenge you to honestly assess your state without falling into self-deception or hopelessness?
?
Wasil claimed to mediate between two groups, but his underlying belief still led to eternal punishment. In what area of your life do you present a middle-ground position while still holding a hardline view in your heart? Will you repent from hypocrisy and align your outward stance with your inner conviction?
?

The Origin of the Created Qur'an Doctrine and Jahm's Excess
Ibn Asakir traces the doctrine of the created Qur'an to Ja'd ibn Dirham and Jahm's extreme negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical origin of the created Qur'an doctrine and the theological error of Jahm's extreme negation of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Heresy often arises as an overreaction to another heresy.

This passage from Ibn Asakir traces the origins of two interconnected doctrines: the createdness of the Qur'an and the extreme denial of divine attributes. It shows how Jahm ibn Safwan combined these ideas and how his position was a reaction against anthropomorphist tendencies. Understanding this history helps us see the importance of balanced theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify key figures
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the individuals mentioned: Ja'd ibn Dirham, Jahm ibn Safwan, al-Harith ibn Surayj, and Muqatil ibn Sulayman.
Notice how each figure contributes to the narrative.
2
Understand the political and theological context
The passage mentions al-Harith ibn Surayj's rebellion and Muqatil's anthropomorphism. Consider how these factors influenced Jahm's position.
Jahm's extreme negation was not purely theological; it had political and polemical dimensions.
3
Recognize the critical distinction missed by Jahm
The passage ends by noting Jahm's failure to distinguish between sharing in a name and sharing in the meaning. Reflect on why this distinction is crucial for orthodox theology.
For example, both God and humans are described as 'alive' (sharing the name), but the meaning is utterly different.
4
Connect to broader theological principles
Consider how this passage illustrates the danger of extremes in theology. The balanced approach is to affirm attributes without comparison to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأول من عرف بالقول بخلق القرآن الجعد بن درهم بدمشق، وكان جهم أخذ ذلك القول من الجعد وضمه إلى بدعه التي قام باذاعتها ومن جملتها نفى الخلود، ولما قام الحارث بن سریج بخراسان ضد الأموية داعيا إلى الكتاب والسنة اعتضد بجهم، وكان مقاتل بن سليمان هناك نحلته في التجسيم فأخذ جهم يرد عليه وينفى مايثبته مقاتل فأفرط في النفى حتى قال ( أن الله لا يوصف بما يوصف به العباد ، ولم يفرق بين الاشتراك فى الاسم والاشتراك فى المعنى
And the first known to say the Qur'an is created was Ja'd ibn Dirham in Damascus. Jahm took that saying from Ja'd and added it to his innovations which he propagated, among them the denial of everlastingness (eternal punishment?). When al-Harith ibn Surayj rose in Khurasan against the Umayyads, calling to the Book and the Sunna, he allied with Jahm. Muqatil ibn Sulayman was there, his doctrine being anthropomorphism, so Jahm began to refute him and deny what Muqatil affirmed, and he went to excess in denial until he said: 'God cannot be described by what describes the servants,' and he did not distinguish between sharing in the name and sharing in the meaning.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage traces the historical origin of two controversial doctrines: the createdness of the Qur'an and the extreme negation of divine attributes. It begins with Ja'd ibn Dirham, who first said the Qur'an is created. Jahm took this from him and combined it with other innovations, including the denial of eternal punishment (nafy al-khulud). The political context is the rebellion of al-Harith ibn Surayj, who allied with Jahm. The theological context is the conflict with Muqatil ibn Sulayman, who held anthropomorphic views. In refuting Muqatil, Jahm went to the opposite extreme, denying any attribute that would describe creatures. The passage ends by noting his failure to distinguish between sharing in a name (ishtirak fi l-ism) and sharing in the meaning (ishtirak fi l-ma'na), a critical distinction in Islamic theology.

Key Terms
خلق القرآن
الجعد بن درهم
نفي الخلود
التجسيم
الاشتراك في الاسم
الاشتراك في المعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jahm's extreme negation was a purely theological position independent of historical circumstances.
Correction: The passage shows it was a reaction to Muqatil's anthropomorphism and involved political alliances with al-Harith ibn Surayj.

Checkpoint
What distinction did Jahm fail to make according to the passage?
A) Between the eternal and the temporal
B) Between sharing in a name and sharing in the meaning
C) Between faith and disbelief
D) Between the Qur'an and other scripture

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of the created Qur'an originated with Ja'd ibn Dirham and was combined by Jahm with other innovations, leading to an extreme negation of divine attributes that failed to distinguish between names and meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you react against someone's error in belief or practice, do you ever swing to the opposite extreme, losing the balanced truth? How can you guard against overcorrection in your own heart and mind?
?
Jahm failed to distinguish between sharing in a name and sharing in a meaning. Are there any attributes of God that you unconsciously imagine as similar to human qualities? How can you affirm them rightly without falling into anthropomorphism or total negation?
?
The passage shows that political alliances and personal reactions shaped theological positions. Are there any beliefs you hold that might be influenced more by your social environment, loyalties, or reactions than by the Qur'an and Sunnah? What would it take to purify your conviction?

The Rise of Heresy and the Caliph's Commission
Ibn Asakir describes the rise of heresy and al-Mahdi's commission to theologians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical context of heresy in the early Abbasid period and how Caliph al-Mahdi mobilized theologians, especially the Mu'tazila, to defend Islam.

Core Idea
When conquests slowed, heresy grew; the caliph's order turned theologians into defenders of faith.

As the early Islamic conquests waned, people turned to intellectual pursuits—but some pursued destructive ideas. Figures like Ibn al-Muqaffa' and others actively spread heresy among Muslims and translated dualist texts. Caliph al-Mahdi responded by commissioning scholars to write refutations, marking a pivotal moment for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the source passage carefully. Notice the names of heretics and the description of their activities. Then read the translation to ensure full comprehension.
The passage uses terms like 'الملاحدة' and 'الثنوية' for heretics and dualists.
2
Identify the historical shift
Look for the phrase 'وبعد أن ابتدأ يطرأ بعض فتور على الفتوح' which indicates a decline in conquests. Ask: How did this shift affect people's preoccupations?
3
Focus on al-Mahdi's response
Note the caliph's command to theologians to compose refutations. Reflect on why the state would intervene in theological matters.
This shows the intersection of politics and theology.
4
Understand the Mu'tazila's role
The passage states that the Mu'tazila were the ones who carried out this task. Consider why they were chosen or stepped forward.
The Mu'tazila were known for their rational approach to theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد أن ابتدأ يطرأ بعض فتور على الفتوح ازداد الناس تفرغا لتلك الآراء المبثوثة، وتغلب على عقولهم شهوة التعمق فيها وأخد أمثال ابن المقفع وحماد عجرد ويحيى بن زياد ومطيع بن اياس وعبد الكريم بن أبى العوجاء (1) يواصلون السعى فى نشر الالحاد بين المسلمين وترجمة كتب الملاحدة والثنوية من الفرس حتى استفحل ،أمرهم، فأمر المهدى علماء المتكلمين بتصنيف الكتب في الرد على الملحدين، فأقاموا الجدل من البراهين وأزالوا الشبه وأوضحوا الحق وخدموا الدين. وكان القائمون بأعباء تلك المدافعات طائفة من المعتزلة فأصبحوا بين عدوين عدو محتال من خارج الملة له آراء وفلسفة تدرب عليها من عهد . قديم وعدو مجاف في داخل الأمة كاد السواد ان ينحاز إليه لتقشفه وهو بعيد عن قضايا العقول راجت عليه تمويهات المضلين من اليهود والثنوية قصاری عمله الوقيعة في أهل النظر لا يفرق بين العدو والحميم، ولو وكل إليه الأمر لما أمكن ان يدافع ساعة من نهار فاشتغل هؤلاء النظار بالأول وتغاضوا عن الثاني حتى أتمو الرد على الزنادقة وكشفوا عن تمويهاتهم، ثم نقضوا كلام الحشوية وأظهروا سخف آرائهم .. وقد علق بنفوس هؤلاء النظار مالا يستهان به من امراض عقلية عدت إليهم من مناظريهم، وكان غالب الفقهاء وحملة السنة طول هذه المكافحات يأبون الخوض في تلك المسائل ويجرون على ما عليه الصحابة وخيار التابعين من الاقتصار على ماثبت من كان ربيب حماد بن سلمة وكان اعترف انه وضع أربعة آلاف حديث . وقد راج ما دس منها في كتب ابيه لأمه بعدما خرف بين كثير من الرواة ثم صارت حججا يتمسك بها الحشوية في معتقدهم
And after some slackening began to occur in the conquests, people became more free to occupy themselves with those spreading opinions, and the desire to delve into them overcame their minds. The likes of Ibn al-Muqaffa', Hammad 'Ajrad, Yahya ibn Ziyad, Mut'i ibn Iyas, and 'Abd al-Karim ibn Abi al-'Awja' continued striving to spread heresy among the Muslims and translate the books of the heretics and dualists from the Persians until their matter became serious. So al-Mahdi ordered the theologians to compose books in refutation of the heretics. They established argumentation based on proofs, removed doubts, clarified the truth, and served the religion. Those who undertook these defenses were a group of the Mu'tazila, so they found themselves between two enemies: a deceptive enemy from outside the faith, with views and philosophy he had trained in from ancient times, and a hostile enemy within the nation, whom the masses almost gravitated to because of his asceticism, though he was far from rational issues, and the deceptions of the misguided Jews and dualists worked on him; his utmost effort was to slander the people of reasoned inquiry, not distinguishing between enemy and friend. Had the matter been left to him, he could not have defended for an hour of the day. So these practitioners of reasoned inquiry occupied themselves with the first (external enemy) and overlooked the second until they completed the refutation of the heretics and exposed their deceptions. Then they demolished the speech of the Hashwiyya and showed the foolishness of their views. And there clung to the souls of these rationalists no insignificant mental diseases that came to them from their opponents. Most of the fuqaha' and carriers of the Sunna throughout these struggles refused to delve into those issues and continued upon what the Companions and the best of the Followers followed, restricting themselves to what was established. (One) was a foster child of Hammad ibn Salama and admitted he fabricated four thousand hadith. What he smuggled spread in the books of his maternal uncle after he became senile among many narrators, then they became proofs that the Hashwiyya cling to in their creed.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a historical account of the Mu'tazila's defense of Islam during the early Abbasid period. It begins with the rise of heresy as conquests slowed, allowing figures like Ibn al-Muqaffa' and others to spread heretical ideas and translate dualist texts. Caliph al-Mahdi responded by commissioning theologians to write refutations. The Mu'tazila took up this task, facing two distinct enemies: the external enemy (dualists, heretics) and an internal enemy (the Hashwiyya, who rejected rational theology and relied on forged hadith). The passage criticizes the internal enemy for being resistant to reason and for slandering the rational theologians. It then describes how the Mu'tazila first dealt with the external threat, then turned to refute the Hashwiyya. Interestingly, it notes that the rationalists themselves were affected by mental illnesses transmitted from their disputants. The passage ends by attributing the reliability of Hashwiyya's proofs to a known hadith forger, emphasizing their reliance on weak or fabricated material.

Key Terms
الملاحدة
الثنوية
المهدي
الزنادقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila initiated theological debates on their own without state support.
Correction: The passage shows that the caliph ordered the theologians to write refutations, indicating state sponsorship.

Checkpoint
What did Caliph al-Mahdi order the theologians to do?
A) Conquer new lands
B) Compose books refuting heretics
C) Translate Greek philosophy
D) Lead the Friday prayers

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's theological defense was prompted by a state response to the rise of heresy after the conquests slowed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your life becomes less occupied with major tasks or struggles, do you find yourself drawn to questions and ideas that pull you away from Allah or toward Him? What will you actively do to guard your free time for faith?
?
The heretics worked tirelessly to spread their ideas through translation and persuasion. Compare your commitment to learning and defending the truth to their zeal. What specific change will you make today to be more proactive in seeking and upholding sound knowledge?
?
The caliph commissioned theologians to refute heresy. If you encounter doubts or false arguments, do you rely on others to answer them, or have you equipped yourself to respond? What is one step you will take this week to strengthen your ability to defend your belief in Allah?

The Mu'tazila's Two Enemies and the Hashwiyya's Flawed Foundation
Ibn Asakir details the Mu'tazila's two enemies and criticizes the Hashwiyya's use of forged hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's dual struggle against external heretics and internal traditionalists (Hashwiyya) and how the latter's reliance on fabricated hadith undermined their position.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila fought two enemies: external dualists and internal Hashwiyya, who used forged hadith.

The Mu'tazila found themselves caught between two very different foes: the sophisticated dualists from outside Islam and the rigid traditionalists within. While they successfully refuted the external heretics, they had to then turn to the internal threat of the Hashwiyya, whose views were based on weak or fabricated hadith. This passage also reveals a remarkable self-criticism of the rationalists and a devastating critique of the Hashwiyya's sources.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and its translation
Read the remaining Arabic passage from 'وكان القائمون بأعباء تلك المدافعات' to the end. Follow the translation carefully.
Pay attention to the description of the two enemies.
2
Identify the two enemies
List the characteristics of each enemy as given in the text. The external enemy has philosophy; the internal enemy is ascetic but irrational and slanders the rationalists.
The internal enemy is the Hashwiyya, a traditionalist group.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila's strategy
Notice the order of engagement: first the external enemy, then the internal. Why did they prioritize the external?
The text says they 'occupied themselves with the first and overlooked the second'.
4
Understand the critique of the Hashwiyya
The passage ends by revealing that the Hashwiyya's proofs came from a known hadith forger. Reflect on the importance of hadith authentication.
The forger admitted fabricating 4,000 hadith.

The Mu'tazila's Two Fronts: External Heretics and Internal Anthropomorphists
Ibn Asakir describes the Mu'tazila's dual challenge from external heretics and internal Hashwiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the strategic position of the Mu'tazila as defenders of Islamic theology, facing both external heretics and internal anti-rationalists.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila faced a dual threat: external heretics and internal anti-rationalists.

Imam Ibn Asakir provides a historical snapshot of the Mu'tazila’s challenging role. They were the primary defenders of Islamic thought, but found themselves squeezed between two very different enemies. This passage explains their strategic choice to first refute the external heretics before turning to the internal opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to understand the situation described.
Notice the descriptive language used for each enemy.
2
Identify the two enemies
Find the two enemies mentioned: one from outside the religion and one from within.
The external enemy is described as a 'scheming enemy' with philosophy; the internal enemy is 'harsh' and ascetic.
3
Analyze the internal enemy
Note how the internal enemy (Hashwiyya) is characterized as irrational and hostile to rational theology.
Ibn Asakir says they denigrate 'people of reflection'.
4
Understand the Mu'tazila's strategy
Consider why they prioritized the external threat first, then turned to the internal one.
The passage says they 'occupied themselves with the first and overlooked the second until they completed the refutation of the heretics'.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Think about how this illustrates the tensions in Islamic theological history between rationalism and literalism.
The Mu'tazila are portrayed as defenders against both heresy and anti-rationalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان القائمون بأعباء تلك المدافعات طائفة من المعتزلة فأصبحوا بين عدوين عدو محتال من خارج الملة له آراء وفلسفة تدرب عليها من عهد قديم وعدو مجاف في داخل الأمة كاد السواد ان ينحاز إليه لتقشفه وهو بعيد عن قضايا العقول راجت عليه تمويهات المضلين من اليهود والثنوية قصاری عمله الوقيعة في أهل النظر لا يفرق بين العدو والحميم، ولو وكل إليه الأمر لما أمكن ان يدافع ساعة من نهار فاشتغل هؤلاء النظار بالأول وتغاضوا عن الثاني حتى أتمو الرد على الزنادقة وكشفوا عن تمويهاتهم، ثم نقضوا كلام الحشوية وأظهروا سخف آرائهم
And those who bore the burden of those defenses were a group of the Mu'tazila. So they found themselves between two enemies: a scheming enemy from outside the religion having views and philosophy he had trained upon since ancient times, and a harsh enemy from within the nation, to whom the masses almost inclined because of his asceticism, while he was far from rational issues, the obfuscations of the misleaders from the Jews and dualists were sold to him, his utmost work was denigrating the people of reflection, not distinguishing between enemy and friend. Had the matter been entrusted to him, he could not have defended for an hour of the day. So these reflective ones occupied themselves with the first and overlooked the second until they completed the refutation of the heretics and exposed their obfuscations, then they refuted the speech of the Hashwiyya and showed the frivolity of their opinions.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage explains the difficult position of the Mu'tazila, who were the primary defenders of Islamic theology. They faced two enemies: one external (outside Islam) who had well-developed philosophical views, and one internal (the Hashwiyya anthropomorphists) who were ascetic but irrational and hostile to rational theology. The Mu'tazila strategically chose to focus on the external threat first, ignoring the internal one until they had refuted the heretics (zanadiqa). Only then did they turn to rebutting the Hashwiyya, exposing the weakness of their arguments. The description of the internal enemy highlights their anti-rationalism and their tendency to slander the people of reflection (ahl al-nazar).

Key Terms
Mu'tazila
Hashwiyya
zindiq
ahl al-nazar

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila were only focused on internal opponents.
Correction: This passage shows they prioritized the external threat first.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, why did the Mu'tazila first engage the external enemy before the internal one?
A) The internal enemy was weaker
B) The external enemy had no real threat
C) The external enemy had dangerous philosophy and needed immediate refutation
D) The Hashwiyya requested delay

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila strategically focused on the external heretical threat before addressing internal theological errors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a challenge to your faith, do you automatically attack the outside skeptic or first examine the internal intellectual shortcuts you may be taking? Which would be harder for you to confront honestly?
?
The Hashwiyya gained the masses' loyalty through their asceticism. Are you more drawn to religious figures because of their outward piety or because of the soundness of their arguments? Could you overlook a flawed theology if the person seems devoted?
?
The Mu'tazila chose a deliberate sequence: external first, then internal. In your own struggle against disobedience, do you tend to fix outward actions before addressing deeper, more comfortable internal attitudes? What internal enemy have you been neglecting while fighting a more visible one?
