The Mihna: A Trial of Faith Under the Abbasids
Describes the mihna under al-Ma'mun, al-Mu'tasim, al-Wathiq, and its end under al-Mutawakkil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical persecution of the Ahl al-Sunna through the mihna, how the Mu'tazila influenced the caliphs, and the role of Imam Ahmad and al-Mutawakkil.

Core Idea
The mihna was a trial of faith that ended by a caliph with mixed deeds.

After establishing the background of sectarian conflict, Ibn 'Asakir now narrates the infamous mihna—the inquisition that tested the Sunni community. This passage traces the support of the Mu'tazila by al-Ma'mun, the continuation of persecution under his successors, and the steadfastness of Imam Ahmad. It also offers a nuanced view of al-Mutawakkil, who ended the mihna but was himself a flawed ruler.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the sequence of caliphs and their actions.
Pay attention to the footnote on denial of vision.
2
Identify the caliphs and their policies
List al-Ma'mun, al-Mu'tasim, al-Wathiq, and al-Mutawakkil. For each, note what they did regarding the mihna and the Mu'tazila.
Al-Wathiq added the denial of seeing Allah.
3
Reflect on Imam Ahmad's role
Consider the phrase 'such steadfastness as raised his stature'. Why is this significant for Sunni identity?
Imam Ahmad became a symbol of resistance.
4
Evaluate al-Mutawakkil
The author says al-Mutawakkil had no other praiseworthy deed besides ending the mihna, and was a Nasibi. How does this balance our view of him?
The passage warns against one-dimensional views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففى مثل هذه الظروف تولى المأمون وأخذ يشايع المعتزلة ويقربهم حتى حمل الناس على القول بخلق القرآن والتنزيه حسبما يوحى إليه عقله وعقول خلطائه، ودام الامتحان طول خلافة المعتصم والواثق وزاد الأخير مسألة نفى الرؤية (۱) فلقى خصوم المعتزلة شدائد استمرت إلى ان رفع المتوكل المحنة وأظهر الأمام أحمد فيها من الثبات ما رفع شأنه، ولم يكن للمتوكل ما يحمد عليه غير رفعه المحنة ومنع الناس عن المناظرات في الآراء والمذاهب . وكان ناصبيا يبغض عليا كرم الله وجهه وله من الأفعال ما لا يخطر بالبال .
In such circumstances, al-Ma'mun assumed power and began to support the Mu'tazila and bring them close, until he compelled people to say that the Qur'an is created and (to adopt) transcendence according to what his mind and the minds of his associates suggested to him. The inquisition lasted throughout the caliphates of al-Mu'tasim and al-Wathiq, and the latter added the issue of denying the vision [of Allah] (1). So the opponents of the Mu'tazila encountered hardships that continued until al-Mutawakkil lifted the inquisition, and Imam Ahmad displayed in it such steadfastness as raised his stature. Al-Mutawakkil had nothing praiseworthy except lifting the inquisition and preventing people from debates on opinions and sects. And he was a Nasibi who hated 'Ali - may Allah honor his face - and he committed acts that do not come to mind.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative describing the political and theological conflict during the Abbasid caliphate. It begins by stating the context: in such circumstances, al-Ma'mun came to power and supported the Mu'tazila, forcing people to accept the doctrine of the created Qur'an and a purely transcendent concept of God based on his own reasoning and that of his associates. The inquisition (mihna) continued under al-Mu'tasim and al-Wathiq, with al-Wathiq adding the denial of the vision of Allah (ru'ya). Opponents of the Mu'tazila faced severe trials until al-Mutawakkil ended the mihna. Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal's steadfastness elevated his status. The author then notes that al-Mutawakkil had no other praiseworthy deed besides ending the mihna and forbidding debates. Moreover, he was a Nasibi (one who harbors enmity towards 'Ali) and engaged in indescribable acts. The passage sets the stage for the subsequent rise of the Hashwiyya and the role of al-Ash'ari.

Key Terms
المحنة (al-mihna)
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
خلق القرآن (khalq al-Qur'an)
نفي الرؤية (nafy al-ru'ya)
أحمد بن حنبل (Ahmad ibn Hanbal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Mutawakkil was a great caliph solely for ending the mihna.
Correction: The passage notes he only had that one praiseworthy deed, but he was a Nasibi who hated 'Ali and committed great evils.

Checkpoint
What did al-Wathiq add to the mihna?
A) The denial of the createdness of the Qur'an
B) The denial of the vision of Allah
C) The prohibition of debates
D) The promotion of the Hashwiyya

Key Takeaway
The mihna tested the Sunni community, Imam Ahmad exemplified steadfastness, and al-Mutawakkil ended it but had serious faults.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were living under the mihna and a caliph demanded you profess the createdness of the Qur'an, would you have the courage to refuse like Imam Ahmad, or would you conform to avoid persecution? What is the one belief you hold so dearly that you would endure hardship for it?
?
Al-Mutawakkil ended the mihna but was a Nasibi who hated Ali and committed heinous acts. How do you reconcile good deeds with a person's serious faults? In your own life, are you quick to overlook your sins because of some good you do, or do you confront them?
?
Imam Ahmad's steadfastness raised his stature among the Sunni community. In what area of your faith are you most tempted to compromise for worldly benefit? What pressure—social, financial, or political—could cause you to abandon a truth you believe in?

Rise of the Hashwiyya and Their Anthropomorphic Innovations
Describes rise of Hashwiyya and lists their anthropomorphic beliefs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Hashwiyya rose after the mihna and what anthropomorphic attributes they wrongly affirmed about Allah.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya attributed movement, direction, and limitation to Allah—rejected by reason and revelation.

After the mihna, a reaction set in: the Hashwiyya gained prominence while the rational theologians were suppressed. This passage describes their rise and the false attributes they ascribed to Allah, showing how they deviated from orthodox Sunni teachings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic and its translation to get the overall sense of the historical context and the list of anthropomorphic attributes.
Notice the contrast between the Hashwiyya's noise and the quiet work of Ahl al-Sunna.
2
Identify the three groups
The passage mentions the Hashwiyya, Nawasib, and Mu'tazila. Understand who they are and the shift in their status after the mihna.
The Hashwiyya are anthropomorphists; Nawasib are those hostile to Ali; Mu'tazila are rational theologians.
3
List the anthropomorphic attributes
Find all the attributes the Hashwiyya affirmed for Allah: movement, transference, limit, direction, sitting, etc. Make a list.
Each attribute is condemned as violating both sharia and reason.
4
Note the sources of these ideas
The passage says they inherited these ideas from dualists and People of the Book. Understand this accusation.
The author traces their doctrines to external influences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ابتدأ رد الفعل يأخذ سيره الطبيعي من ارتفاع شأن الحشوية والنواصب وانقماع أهل النظر والمعتزلة . وأهل السنة والمحدثين يواصلون العمل فى علومهم فى غير جلبة ولا ضوضاء، والحشوية يجرون على طيشهم وعمايتهم واستتباعهم الرعاع والغوغاء ويتقولون في الله مالا يجوزه الشرع ولا العقل من اثبات الحركة له والنقلة والحد والجهة والقعود والاقعاد والاستلقاء والاستقرار، إلى نحوها مما تلقوه بالقبول من دجاجلة الملبسين من الثنوية وأهل الكتاب ومما ورثوه من أمم قد خلت ويؤلفون في ذلك كتبا يملأونها بالوقيعة في الآخرين ويخرقون حجاب الهيبة في الاكفار متبرقعين بالسنة ومعتزين إلى السلف يستغلون ما ينقل عن بعض السلف من الأقوال المجملة التي لاحجة فيها. نعم لهم سلف ولكن من غير هذه الأمة وهم على سنة ولكن على من سنها الأوزار إلى يوم القيامة .
Then the reaction began to take its natural course: the status of the Hashwiyya and the Nawasib rose, and the people of reflection and the Mu'tazila were suppressed. The Ahl al-Sunna and the hadith scholars continued their work in their sciences without noise or clamor. The Hashwiyya persisted in their recklessness and blindness and in attracting the rabble and mobs, and they fabricated lies about Allah what neither the Law nor reason permits: affirming movement, transference, limit, direction, sitting, making sit, reclining, settling, and the like, which they accepted from the charlatans of the dualists and the People of the Book, and what they inherited from nations that have passed. They compose books filled with vilification of others and tear down the veil of reverence in declaring disbelief (takfir), cloaking themselves in the Sunna and claiming allegiance to the Salaf, exploiting general statements transmitted from some of the Salaf that have no proof. Yes, they have predecessors, but not from this nation; and they are upon a sunna, but upon the one who instituted it lie the burdens until the Day of Judgment.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage narrates the aftermath of the mihna. A natural reaction occurred: the Hashwiyya (anthropomorphists) and Nawasib (those hostile to 'Ali) rose in status, while the rational theologians (ahl al-nazar) and Mu'tazila were suppressed. Meanwhile, the Ahl al-Sunna and hadith scholars quietly continued their work. The Hashwiyya, described as reckless and blind, attracted the ignorant masses and attributed to Allah things forbidden by both revelation and reason: movement, transference, limit, direction, sitting, making sit, reclining, settling, etc. These ideas they inherited from earlier corrupt groups like the dualists and People of the Book. They authored books filled with slander and readily declared others disbelievers, while pretending to follow the Sunna and Salaf, exploiting ambiguous statements of early Muslims that lack clear proof. The author then makes a striking point: they do have predecessors, but not from this Muslim community; and they follow a 'sunna' (path) that will bring its inventor burdens on Judgment Day. This passage highlights the theological deviation of the Hashwiyya and criticizes their methodology.

Key Terms
الحشوية (al-Hashwiyya)
النواصب (al-Nawasib)
الرعاع (al-ru'aa')
الغوغاء (al-ghawgha')
الحركة (al-haraka)
الجهة (al-jiha)
الثنوية (al-thunawiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which group did the Hashwiyya allegedly inherit their anthropomorphic ideas from?
A) The Mu'tazila
B) The dualists and People of the Book
C) The Ahl al-Sunna
D) The Salaf directly

Key Takeaway
The Hashwiyya innovated anthropomorphic attributes for Allah, drawing from external sources and contradicting Islamic teachings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself imagining Allah sitting on a throne like a king moves or changes position? After reading this lesson, how must you correct that image in your heart? What specific prayer will you say to ask Allah to purify your belief?
?
The Hashwiyya attracted the rabble by telling them what they wanted to hear. Are there any comfortable beliefs about Allah that you hold because they feel good rather than because they are true? Will you commit to studying the Quran and Sunnah to uproot any trace of anthropomorphism from your creed?
?
The author traces these false attributes to foreign sources. What ideas about Allah have you absorbed from your culture or environment that might contradict Islamic teachings? What is the first action you will take this week to verify your understanding of Allah's attributes against the Quran and authentic hadith?

Spread of Ash'ari Madhhab Through Scholarship, Not Rulers
Ibn Asakir enumerates the scholarly carriers of Ash'arism and argues that its spread was due to knowledge, not political force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari madhhab spread through scholarly authority rather than political coercion, as demonstrated by Ibn Asakir's historical account.

Core Idea
Ash'arism spread by knowledge, not by swords.

Ibn Asakir presents a historical argument that the Ash'ari madhhab's expansion was driven by the efforts of scholars, not the power of rulers. He traces its spread across regions and contrasts it with the behavior of factions like the Hashwiyya, showing that true authority lies in knowledge. This passage defends the Ash'ari legacy against claims of political imposition.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Asakir's historical argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the narrative flow from scholarly propagation to political tolerance.
Notice the listing of key scholars and regions.
2
Identify the key scholars and regions
Identify the scholars mentioned and their roles in spreading Ash'arism in Hijaz, Syria, etc.
Note the mention of Abu Dharr al-Harawi and Abd al-Aziz al-Tabari.
3
Understand the contrast with Hashwiyya
Examine how Ibn Asakir describes the Hashwiyya and their innovations, and how rulers like Salah al-Din treated them.
Consider why Ibn Asakir includes this detail.
4
Analyze the conclusion
Read the concluding statement about 'sultan al-ilm' vs 'shaukat al-salatine' and reflect on its significance for Ash'ari identity.
This is the core thesis of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وابي الحسن القابسي وابى الوليد بن الباجي وأبي بكر بن العربي تلامذتهم أياد بيضاء في ذلك، وقام بنشر المذهب في الحجاز راوية الجامع الصحيح الحافظ ابو ذر الهروى وأخذ عنه من ارتحل اليه من علماء الآفاق، وكان انتشاره بالشام قبل ذلك بواسطة صاحب الاشعری ابی الحسن عبد العزيز الطبري راوية تفسير ابن جرير عن مؤلفه وكان اهل الشام يجتلبون عـبــار الائمة من المذهب الأشعرى حيناً بعد حين كالامام قطب الدين النيسابورى اجتلبه نور الدين الشهيد على طلب العلماء. وكان جماعة من المقادسة الحنابلة ممن ورثوا بعض آراء ابن كرام الذى كان عشش بالقدس وباض وترك أصحابا له متقشفين يتوارثها منهم من بعدهم هاجروا منها لما احتلها النصارى وحملوا بدع التشبيه الى الشام وكان بها شئ من تلك البدع من عهد عبد الواحد الشيرازي صاحب أبي يعلى وكان السلطان صلاح الدين الايوبى يرعى خاطرهم لكونهم مهاجرين زهادا ويتغاضى عن معتقدهم، ولم يكن يحمل الناس علي المذهب الأشعرى كما ظن بل كان الواعظ ابن نجية الحنبلى المشهور مقربا ،عنده ومجافاته القاسية مع الامام الشهاب الطوسى القائم بنصرة الاشعرى بمصر تجرى على منظر منه ومسمع ويسكت عن ذلك بل كاد آله أن ينحازوا اليهم فى المعتقد لولا وقفة الأمام عز الدين بن عبد السلام فى هذه المسألة وقفة عالم يقوم بواجبه فتضاءات أصواتهم وانجمعوا في ديورهم واقتصروا على الروايات، فيظهر من جميع ذلك ان انتشار المذهب الأشعرى في البلاد بسلطان العلم لا بشوكة السلاطين، وما وقع ببغداد وغيرها من بعض التشدد على الحشوية بين حين و آخر فلإخلالهم الأمن وإحداثهم القلاقل.
And Abu al-Hasan al-Qabisi, and Abu al-Walid ibn al-Baji, and Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi, their students, had white hands [great favors] in that. And the spread of the madhhab in the Hijaz was undertaken by the transmitter of the Sahih [al-Bukhari], the hafiz Abu Dharr al-Harawi, and those scholars from the horizons who traveled to him took from him. And its spread in Syria before that was through the companion of al-Ash'ari, Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Aziz al-Tabari, the transmitter of Tafsir Ibn Jarir from its author. And the people of Syria would bring the [؟] of the imams of the Ash'ari madhhab from time to time, like Imam Qutb al-Din al-Naysaburi, whom Nur al-Din al-Shahid brought at the request of scholars. And there was a group of the Hanbalis of Jerusalem who inherited some opinions of Ibn Karram, who had nested in Jerusalem and laid eggs and left ascetic companions who inherited them from those after them; they emigrated from there when the Christians occupied it and carried the innovations of anthropomorphism to Syria, and there was something of those innovations from the time of Abd al-Wahid al-Shirazi, the companion of Abu Ya'la. And Sultan Salah al-Din al-Ayyubi would take care of their feelings because they were emigrants and ascetics, and he would overlook their creed. And he did not force people to the Ash'ari madhhab as is thought; rather, the preacher Ibn Najiyya al-Hanbali, the famous one, was close to him, and his harsh aversion to Imam Shihab al-Tusi, who stood for the support of al-Ash'ari in Egypt, occurred in his sight and hearing, and he remained silent about that; rather, his family almost inclined to them in creed had it not been for the stance of Imam Izz al-Din ibn Abd al-Salam in this matter, a stance of a scholar doing his duty, so their voices faded and they gathered in their homes and confined themselves to narrations. So it appears from all that that the spread of the Ash'ari madhhab in the lands was by the authority of knowledge, not by the edge of the sultans, and what happened in Baghdad and elsewhere of some severity against the Hashwiyya from time to time was due to their breaching security and causing disturbances.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a historical argument demonstrating that the Ash'ari madhhab spread through scholarly authority, not political force. Ibn Asakir begins by listing key scholars who propagated the madhhab: Abu al-Hasan al-Qabisi, Abu al-Walid al-Baji, and Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi, and their students. In the Hijaz, it was spread by the hadith scholar Abu Dharr al-Harawi. In Syria, it spread earlier through Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Aziz al-Tabari, a companion of al-Ash'ari. Later, rulers like Nur al-Din would bring Ash'ari scholars at the request of scholars. He then contrasts this with the Hashwiyya (anthropomorphists), who had some presence, and notes that Sultan Salah al-Din tolerated them but did not force Ash'arism on anyone. Ibn Asakir mentions the famous Hanbali preacher Ibn Najiyya being close to Salah al-Din, and that the sultan remained silent when Ibn Najiyya harshly opposed the Ash'ari scholar Shihab al-Tusi. Only when Izz al-Din ibn Abd al-Salam took a firm stand did the Hashwiyya's voices fade. The conclusion: Ash'arism spread by the authority of knowledge, not by the power of sultans, and any harshness against the Hashwiyya was due to their own disruptive behavior.

Key Terms
المذهب الأشعرى
سلطان العلم
شوكة السلاطين
الحشوية
تفسير ابن جرير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the Ash'ari madhhab was spread through political coercion and the power of rulers.
Correction: Ibn Asakir demonstrates that it was spread by the 'authority of knowledge' (sultan al-ilm) through scholars, and rulers like Salah al-Din did not force it upon people.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, what was the primary reason for the spread of the Ash'ari madhhab?
A) The support of Sultan Salah al-Din al-Ayyubi
B) The authority of knowledge (sultan al-ilm)
C) The use of military force
D) The patronage of the Abbasid caliphs

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari madhhab spread through the authority of scholars and knowledge, not through the force of rulers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about a theological position, do you accept it because of the strength of its evidence or because of the status of the person teaching it? How can you train yourself to follow knowledge, not personalities?
?
Think of a time when you were pressured to adopt a certain view by someone in authority. Did you stand by what you believed to be true, or did you yield? What does this lesson teach you about where true authority comes from?
?
If you were a teacher, would you rather have your students follow you because of your knowledge or because of your influence? Are you building your own faith on solid knowledge or on the approval of others? Pledge today to prioritize evidence over convenience.

Madhhabs and the Spread of Ash'ari Theology
Ibn Asakir describes which madhhabs adopted Ash'ari theology and the position of Hanbalis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the major Sunni legal schools historically aligned with Ash'ari and Maturidi kalam, and why the Hanbalis had a unique relationship with al-Ash'ari.

Core Idea
Ash'ari theology was adopted unevenly across madhhabs, with Hanbalis both rejecting and partially accepting it.

Ibn Asakir gives a concise picture of how the Ash'ari and Maturidi theological methods were distributed among the four Sunni legal schools. He highlights a tension: al-Ash'ari considered himself a follower of Ahmad ibn Hanbal, yet many Hanbalis—especially the Hashwiyya—strongly opposed him. This passage provides a snapshot of the theological landscape as of the 6th century AH.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the claims
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Observe how Ibn Asakir describes the jurists' attitudes toward al-Ash'ari.
Pay attention to the proportions given for each madhhab.
2
Identify the Hanbali paradox
Notice that al-Ash'ari declared himself a Hanbali in debates, yet the Hanbalis—especially the Hashwiyya—reject him. Why would this be?
The Hashwiyya are a group among Hanbalis opposed to kalam.
3
Trace the distribution
List the fractions: all Malikis, three-quarters of Shafi'is, one-third of Hanafis, and a portion of Hanbalis follow Ash'ari kalam. The other two-thirds of Hanafis follow Maturidi kalam.
Note the exceptions: some Hanafis and Shafi'is inclined to Mu'tazilism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفقهاء المذاهب يتجاذبون الأشعرى إلى مذاهبهم ويترجمونه في طبقاتهم، والحنابلة أحق بذلك حيث يصرح الأشعرى فى مناظراته معهم انه على مذهب احمد لكنهم لا يترجمونه في طبقاتهم ولا يعدونه منهم بل يمقته الحشوية منهم فوق مقت المعتزلة . فالمالكية كافة وثلاثة أرباع الشافعية وثلث الحنفية وقسم من الحنابلة على هذه الطريقة من الكلام من عهد الباقلاني، والثلثان من الحنفية على الطريقة الماتريدية فى ديار ماوراء النهر وبلاد الترك والافغان والهند والصين وما والاها الا من انحاز منهم الى الاعتزال كبعض الشافعية .
And the jurists of the madhhabs draw al-Ash'ari to their madhhabs and include him in their biographical dictionaries. And the Hanbalis are more entitled to that since al-Ash'ari declares in his debates with them that he is on the madhhab of Ahmad, but they do not include him in their biographical dictionaries nor consider him one of them; rather, the Hashwiyya among them hate him more than they hate the Mu'tazila. So all of the Malikis, three-quarters of the Shafi'is, one-third of the Hanafis, and a portion of the Hanbalis follow this method of kalam from the time of al-Baqillani. And two-thirds of the Hanafis follow the Maturidi method in the lands beyond the river and the lands of the Turks, Afghans, India, China, and adjacent areas, except for those among them who inclined to Mu'tazilism, like some of the Shafi'is.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a classification of how the various legal madhhabs relate to Ash'ari theology. Ibn Asakir notes that jurists of all madhhabs try to claim al-Ash'ari as their own, but the Hanbalis have the best claim because al-Ash'ari himself stated in his debates that he followed the madhhab of Ahmad ibn Hanbal. However, the Hanbalis (specifically the Hashwiyya among them) do not accept him; they hate him more than the Mu'tazila. Then Ibn Asakir breaks down the adherence: all Malikis, three-quarters of Shafi'is, one-third of Hanafis, and a portion of Hanbalis follow the Ash'ari kalam method (since al-Baqillani). The other two-thirds of Hanafis follow the Maturidi method, prevalent in Central Asia, Turkey, Afghanistan, India, China, and nearby areas, except those Hanafis who inclined to Mu'tazilism, and also some Shafi'is inclined to Mu'tazilism.

Ibn Asakir demonstrates that while Ash'ari himself was Hanbali in fiqh, the Ash'ari theological method was adopted by most Malikis and Shafi'is but only partially by Hanafis and Hanbalis.

Maliki Madhhab's Immunity from Bid'ah
Ibn Asakir discusses how the Maliki madhhab remained free from innovations due to Malik's prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Malik's prohibition of narrating hadiths of divine attributes protected the Maliki madhhab from innovations like Mu'tazilism and anthropomorphism, and how this parallels Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal's similar policy regarding rebellion hadiths.

Core Idea
Malik's silence on attribute hadiths shielded his school from bid'ah.

Imam Ibn Asakir, in his defense of the Maliki school, highlights a distinctive characteristic: its immunity from harmful innovations. He traces this to Imam Malik's explicit prohibition of narrating certain hadiths about Allah's attributes. This lesson unpacks that insight and its wider implications, including a notable exception.

Guided Path
1
Read and understand the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the central claim that the Maliki madhhab repels innovations, specifically Mu'tazilism and anthropomorphism.
The passage begins with a possible marginal note 'Baybars', which may be disregarded.
2
Analyze the cause and effect
Identify the reason Ibn Asakir gives for this immunity: Malik's prohibition of narrating hadiths of the attributes. Consider how this policy prevented theological speculation.
Malik's approach was to avoid engaging with ambiguous texts, thus avoiding error.
3
Compare with Imam Ahmad's approach
Read the analogy with Imam Ahmad's prohibition of narrating hadiths about rebelling against oppressive rulers. Reflect on the parallel outcome: the caliphs overlooked Hanbali activities.
Also note the exception: some Malikis had Sufi extremism from Ibn Tumart, showing not absolute immunity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيبرس ومن خصائص مذهب عالم المدينة كونه ينفي خبث البدع عن أهل مذهبه فلاتجد بين المالكية بدع الاعتزال والتشبيه ومما أفاد في ذلك على ما احسبه منع مالك رواية اخبار الصفات كما كان احمد يمنع عن رواية احاديث الخروج على ظلمة الولاة فأفاده في تغاضى خلفاء بغداد عن الحنابلة مهما عملوا بل في تقريبهم، نعم يوجد عند بعض المالكية نوع غلو في التصوف من عهد ابن تومرت.
Baybars? And among the characteristics of the madhhab of the scholar of Medina is that it repels the evil of innovations from its people, so you do not find among the Malikis the innovations of Mu'tazilism and anthropomorphism. And among what benefited in that, as I think, is Malik's prohibition of narrating hadiths of the attributes, just as Ahmad prohibited narrating hadiths about rebelling against oppressive rulers, which benefited in the Baghdad caliphs overlooking the Hanbalis no matter what they did, rather even bringing them close. Yes, among some Malikis there is a type of extremism in Sufism from the time of Ibn Tumart.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a reflection on a specific virtue of the Maliki madhhab. Ibn Asakir states that the Maliki school (attributed to the scholar of Medina, i.e., Malik ibn Anas) repels innovations from its followers, so one does not find Mu'tazilism or anthropomorphism among Malikis. He attributes this to Malik's prohibition of narrating hadiths concerning the divine attributes (i.e., ambiguous hadiths about God's attributes). He draws an analogy with Ahmad ibn Hanbal's prohibition of narrating hadiths about rebelling against oppressive rulers, which led the Abbasid caliphs to overlook Hanbali excesses and even bring them close. However, Ibn Asakir notes an exception: some Malikis have a type of extremism in Sufism dating from the time of Ibn Tumart (the founder of the Almohad movement).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maliki madhhab is entirely free from any innovation.
Correction: Ibn Asakir states that while the school is free from Mu'tazilism and anthropomorphism, some Malikis have extremism in Sufism from the time of Ibn Tumart.

Checkpoint

D) The persecution of innovators by rulers

Key Takeaway
Imam Malik's prohibition of narrating ambiguous hadiths of attributes was a key factor in keeping the Maliki school free from major theological innovations, though some later Sufi extremism appeared.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage notes that even a generally protected madhhab can have pockets of extremism. What specific 'Sufi extremism' might be hiding in your own practice or community? Are you willing to identify it and renounce it, even if it feels like criticizing a beloved group?

The Rise and Fall of a Hashwi Movement from Harran
Ibn Asakir narrates the rise and failure of a Hashwi movement from Harran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a Hashwi (anthropomorphist) movement attempted to revive its doctrines under the guise of Salafism and why it ultimately failed due to the rigorous scholarship of Ahl al-Sunna.

Core Idea
False claims to the Salafi mantle cannot withstand the rigorous scholarship of Ahl al-Sunna.

Ibn Asakir provides a historical account of a movement from Harran that tried to reintroduce Hashwi (anthropomorphist) ideas under the pretense of following the Salaf. He contrasts this with the established Ash'ari and Maturidi scholarship, which had already thoroughly refuted such doctrines. This passage illustrates how deviant movements can arise but are ultimately defeated by sound scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the historical context: after the sack of Baghdad, a group from Harran came to Syria.
Pay attention to the key terms: Hashwi, Ahl al-Sunna, and the mention of Ash'aris and Maturidis.
2
Understand the Hanbali landscape before Harran
Identify three groups among the Hanbalis: those who followed tafwid, those who inclined to Mu'tazila, and the majority who were Hashwiyya following Salimi and Karrami methods. Note the impact of al-Zahir's appointment of judges.
This sets the stage for the near-elimination of Hashwiyya before the Harran group arrived.
3
Analyze the Harran man's strategy
The man was intelligent and eloquent, and he gained the trust of scholars. His plan was to replace the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna with Hashwiyya under the guise of the Salaf.
He concealed his true intention, presenting his movement as a return to the Salaf.
4
Identify the reason for his failure
Ibn Asakir highlights two fatal flaws: the overwhelming strength of Ash'ari and Maturidi arguments developed over centuries, and the man's lack of proper training in theoretical sciences, leading to incoherent knowledge.
The phrase 'if someone like him tried to clash with them, he would only fall on his head' emphasizes the inevitability of failure.
5
Reflect on the historical lesson
The movement ended not by his success but by the scholars' rejection. Consider how this illustrates the resilience of Sunni orthodoxy against innovations.
This lesson shows the importance of scholarly consensus and rigorous training.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعض الحنابلة على مسلك السلف في التفويض وترك الخوض وبعضهم انحاز الى المعتزلة، وكان غالبهم على تعاقب القرون حشوية على الطريقة السالمية والكرامية الى ان جعل الظاهر قضاء القضاة في المذاهب الأربعة لاول مرة فاتصلوا بعلماء أهل السنة يفاوضونهم فى العلم فأخذت تزول أمراضهم البدعية وكاد أن لا يبقى بينهم حشوى لولا جالية حران بعد نكبة بغداد حطوا رحلهم بالشام ونبغ من بينهم رجل حسنت نشأته في الطلب على ذكاء وحافظة و سمت وتمكن من اجتلاب ثقة شيوخ العلم الى نفسه وثنائهم عليه وكان واعظاً طلق اللسان فاذا هو يجرى على خطة مدبرة في احلال المذهب الحشوى تحت ستار مذهب السف محل مذهب أهل السنة ولم يعلم أن مذهب أهل السنة من الأشاعرة والماتريدية بلغ من التمحيص العلمي على تعاقب القرون بأيدى نوابغ اهل النظر والفقه فى الدين ممن لا يعد هذا الحشوي من صغار تلامذتهم الى مستوى من قوة الحجج بحيث إذا حاول مثله ان يصطدم بها لا يقع الا علي أم رأسه فيردى ولا يودى وحيث لم يكن له شيخ يرشده في العلوم النظرية اصبح علمه لا يرتكن على شئ وثيق خليطا كثير التناقض توزعت مواهبه في الهواء متعبة ثم افضى الى ما عمل وزالت فتنه برد العلماء عليه
And some Hanbalis follow the path of the Salaf in tafwid [consigning the meaning of ambiguous texts to God] and refraining from delving [into theological debate], and some of them inclined to the Mu'tazila. And most of them, over the succession of centuries, were Hashwiyya on the Salimi and Karrami method, until al-Zahir made the judgeship of judges in the four madhhabs for the first time, so they connected with the scholars of Ahl al-Sunna and negotiated with them in knowledge, so their innovational diseases began to disappear, and there almost remained no Hashwi among them, except for a group from Harran after the sack of Baghdad who set down their saddles in Syria. And among them emerged a man who had a good upbringing in seeking knowledge, with intelligence, memory, and exaltation, and he was able to attract the trust of the shaykhs of knowledge to himself and their praise of him, and he was an eloquent preacher. And so he carried out a premeditated plan to replace the Hashwi madhhab under the guise of the madhhab of the Salaf in place of the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna. And he did not know that the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna from the Ash'aris and Maturidis had reached, through the succession of centuries at the hands of the geniuses of theoretical investigation and jurisprudence in religion, to such a level of strength of arguments that if someone like him tried to clash with them, he would only fall on his head and be destroyed without destroying. And since he had no shaykh to guide him in the theoretical sciences, his knowledge became resting on nothing solid, mixed with much contradiction, his talents scattered in the air, exhausting. Then he came to what he did, and his fitna ended by the scholars' rejection of him.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a historical critique of a specific Hashwi (anthropomorphism) movement from Harran, likely referring to Ibn Taymiyya. Ibn Asakir first describes the Hanbalis: some follow tafwid (consigning ambiguous texts to God), some incline to Mu'tazila, but most were Hashwiyya following the Salimi and Karrami methods. However, when the ruler al-Zahir (Baybars?) established the office of chief judge for each of the four madhhabs, these Hanbalis began to engage with Sunni scholars and their heretical diseases started to fade, nearly eliminating Hashwiyya among them. But after the Mongol sack of Baghdad, a community from Harran came to Syria, and among them arose a man with intelligence and eloquence who secretly planned to replace Sunni theology with Hashwiyya under the guise of the Salaf. Ibn Asakir argues that this man was unaware of the strength of Sunni theology developed over centuries by brilliant scholars; any attempt to clash with it would result in his self-destruction. Moreover, lacking a proper teacher in theoretical sciences, his knowledge was incoherent. Ultimately, his fitna ended through scholarly opposition.

Key Terms
الحشوية
مذهب السلف
أهل السنة
الأشاعرة
الماتريدية
التفويض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this movement was a return to true Salafism, faithfully reviving the way of the early Muslims.
Correction: Ibn Asakir clarifies that the man's plan was to replace the established Sunni orthodoxy with Hashwiyya (anthropomorphism) under the false guise of following the Salaf. The movement was not a genuine return to Salaf but a disguised innovation.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, what was the ultimate fate of the Hashwi from Harran and his movement?
A) It successfully replaced Sunni theology
B) It faded after he gained political power
C) It ended due to scholarly rejection
D) It spread to other regions

Key Takeaway
The strength of Sunni theological scholarship, refined over centuries, ensures that any deviant movement claiming the mantle of the Salaf will be exposed and defeated by scholarly consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what ways might you be attracted to a charismatic preacher or movement that claims to represent 'the way of the Salaf'? How can you test whether such claims are genuine or merely a cover for innovation?
?
Reflect on a time when you held a belief strongly but later realized it was based on flawed or incomplete knowledge. What steps can you take now to ensure your understanding of core Islamic beliefs is built on solid, scholarly foundations?
?
The man from Harran had good qualities—intelligence, memory, eloquence—but lacked a qualified teacher. Are there areas of your faith where you rely on your own intellect or popular speakers rather than seeking out established scholars? What is one concrete change you will make to rectify this?

Origins of Khawarij, Shi'a, Murji'a, and Jabriyya
Ibn Asakir analyzes the non-scientific origins of major sectarian movements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir analyzes the non-scientific origins of the Khawarij, Shi'a, Murji'a, and Jabriyya.

Core Idea
Sectarian origins are often political or reactive, not purely theological.

Ibn Asakir offers a historical analysis of how major sectarian movements emerged. He distinguishes between those born from political passion (Khawarij, Shi'a), those arising as a scholarly reaction (Murji'a), and those from unscientific speculation (Jabriyya). This passage invites us to reflect on the role of knowledge versus emotion in the formation of religious groups.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
The passage is a single paragraph covering four groups.
2
Identify the four groups and their described origins
Find where Ibn Asakir discusses each group. Note the key phrase: 'knowledge had no part' for Khawarij and Shi'a, 'offspring of a kind of scientific research' for Murji'a, and 'resulted from unscientific research' for Jabriyya.
3
Note the infiltration of heretics
Ibn Asakir says that enemies of religion (zanadiqa) infiltrated the Khawarij and Shi'a. Consider what that implies about their development.
This shows external manipulation after initial formation.
4
Reflect on the Jabriyya's description
The Jabriyya are called 'callers to stagnation and harbingers of destruction'. Think about how their origin from unscientific research leads to this characterization.
The passage is cut off at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجلى أنه لا دخل للعلم فى نشأة الخوارج والشيعة بل ولدتهما العاطفة السياسية ثم اندس فيهما خصوم الدين من الزنادقة فتطورتا أطواراً شائنة واتجاههما الأصلى نحو خصومة الحكومة القائمة، والمرجئة وليدة نوع من البحث العلمي اتجاهها نحو معاكسة الخوارج في المعتقد ثم تشعبت منها آراء بعيدة عن الدين والعلم أورثت التهاون فى العمل. والجبرية دعاة الخمود ونذير الدمار نتجت عن بحث غیر علمی علوقها من مجاورة
And it is clear that knowledge had no part in the emergence of the Khawarij and the Shi'a; rather, political passion gave birth to them, then the enemies of religion from the heretics infiltrated them, so they developed into ugly phases, and their original direction was toward opposition to the existing government. And the Murji'a are the offspring of a kind of scientific research, their direction being to oppose the Khawarij in creed, then opinions branched from it far from religion and knowledge, which led to negligence in action. And the Jabriyya, callers to stagnation and harbingers of destruction, resulted from unscientific research, their attachment from proximity.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the origins of four sectarian groups. Ibn Asakir states clearly that the Khawarij and Shi'a were not born from scholarly inquiry but from political emotion. Then heretics (zanadiqa) infiltrated them, leading to ugly developments; their original orientation was opposition to the ruling government. The Murji'a, on the other hand, originated from a kind of scientific research, specifically to counter the Khawarij in creed, but later extreme opinions emerged that led to negligence in action (i.e., disregarding the importance of deeds). Finally, the Jabriyya (fatalists) are described as callers to stagnation and heralds of destruction, arising from unscientific inquiry, with the text cut off at 'their attachment from proximity'.

Key Terms

الشيعة
المرجئة
الجبرية
الزنادقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these sects arose from deep theological debate and scholarly inquiry.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that the Khawarij and Shi'a emerged from political passion, the Murji'a from a reactive scholarly effort that later went astray, and the Jabriyya from unscientific research.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir shows that the origins of major sects were often non-scientific: Khawarij and Shi'a from politics, Murji'a from reactive scholarship, and Jabriyya from flawed speculation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Asakir says the Khawarij and Shi'a were born from political passion, not knowledge. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that are driven more by emotional reaction or political allegiance than by sound scholarship? Can you identify one such belief and commit tonight to re-examining it through the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
The Murji'a began as a scholarly reaction but later branched into negligence in action. Is there any area of your religious knowledge—perhaps in fiqh, tawheed, or hadith—that has made you careless about acting upon it? What is one specific action you have delayed or neglected that you will do tomorrow to correct this?
?
The Jabriyya are called 'callers to stagnation and harbingers of destruction'. When you face a difficult trial, do you ever use 'qadr' as an excuse to remain passive and not strive? How can you replace that passive mindset with active trust in Allah while taking the means He has commanded?

The Maliki School: Purity from Innovation
Ibn Asakir describes the Maliki school's freedom from innovation and attributes it to Malik's prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Asakir considers the Maliki school free from major innovations and how Malik's prohibition on narrating attribute hadiths contributed to this purity.

Core Idea
Malik's ban on attribute hadiths preserved the Maliki school from anthropomorphism and Mu'tazilism.

Ibn Asakir highlights a distinctive virtue of the Maliki school: its followers are generally free from the innovations of Mu'tazila and anthropomorphism. He traces this to Imam Malik's policy of prohibiting the narration of hadiths about God's attributes, which prevented theological speculation. This policy also had an unintended benefit for the Hanbalis, as we will see. However, he notes that some later Malikis fell into Sufi extremism, showing that no school is entirely immune.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Malikis
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice the cause-and-effect reasoning: Malik's prohibition led to purity.
Pay attention to the comparison with Ahmad ibn Hanbal's prohibition.
2
Identify the key claim
Ibn Asakir claims you do not find Mu'tazila or anthropomorphism among Malikis. What is his evidence?
He attributes it to Malik's ban on narrating 'akhbar al-sifat'.
3
Consider the nuance
The author admits that some Malikis later adopted Sufi extremism from Ibn Tumart. How does this qualify the initial claim?
This shows the school's overall tendency despite occasional deviations.
4
Reflect on the broader point
Think about how a school's founding principles can shape its later character. Malik's concern for avoiding anthropomorphism had long-term effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن خصائص مذهب عالم المدينة كونه ينفي خبث البدع عن أهل مذهبه فلاتجد بين المالكية بدع الاعتزال والتشبيه ومما أفاد في ذلك على ما احسبه منع مالك رواية اخبار الصفات كما كان احمد يمنع عن رواية احاديث الخروج على ظلمة الولاة فأفاده في تغاضى خلفاء بغداد عن الحنابلة مهما عملوا بل في تقريبهم، نعم يوجد عند بعض المالكية نوع غلو في التصوف من عهد ابن تومرت. وبعض الحنابلة على مسلك السلف في التفويض وترك الخوض وبعضهم انحاز الى المعتزلة، وكان غالبهم على تعاقب القرون حشوية على الطريقة السالمية والكرامية الى ان جعل الظاهر قضاء القضاة في المذاهب الأربعة لاول مرة فاتصلوا بعلماء أهل السنة يفاوضونهم فى العلم فأخذت تزول أمراضهم البدعية
Among the characteristics of the school of the scholar of Medina (i.e., Malik) is that it repudiates the evil of innovations from its followers, so you do not find among the Malikis the innovations of Mu'tazila and anthropomorphism. And among the benefits of that, as I reckon, is Malik's prohibition of narrating hadiths of the attributes (akhbar al-sifat), just as Ahmad prohibited narrating hadiths about rebelling against unjust rulers, which benefited in the tolerance of the caliphs of Baghdad towards the Hanbalis, whatever they did, indeed in their drawing them near. Yes, there exists among some Malikis a kind of extremism in Sufism from the era of Ibn Tumart. And some Hanbalis follow the salaf in tafwid (consigning the meaning to God) and avoiding engagement [in theological discourse], and some inclined towards Mu'tazila. But most of them over the centuries were Hashwiyya following the Salimiyya and Karramiyya methods, until al-Zahir appointed judgeships in the four schools for the first time, so they connected with Sunni scholars, discussing knowledge with them, and their innovated diseases began to disappear.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage begins by highlighting a distinctive virtue of the Maliki school: its freedom from the innovations of Mu'tazila and anthropomorphism. The author attributes this to Malik's policy of prohibiting the narration of hadiths about God's attributes (which could lead to anthropomorphism), similar to Ahmad ibn Hanbal's prohibition of narrating hadiths about rebellion. This policy led to the Baghdad caliphs tolerating the Hanbalis. However, the author acknowledges that some Malikis later fell into Sufi extremism from the time of Ibn Tumart. The passage then discusses the Hanbali school: some followed the salaf in tafwid (consigning the meaning of ambiguous verses to God) and avoided theology, while others joined the Mu'tazila. But most Hanbalis over the centuries were Hashwiyya, following the Salimiyya and Karramiyya sects, until the caliph al-Zahir established judgeships for all four schools for the first time. This brought the Hanbalis into contact with Sunni scholars, through discussion of knowledge, and their innovated diseases began to disappear. Thus, the author shows the historical evolution of the Hanbali school from a state of being mostly Hashwiyya to being gradually corrected through interaction with mainstream Sunni scholarship.

Key Terms
مالكية
بدع
اعتزال
تشبيه
أخبار الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maliki school was completely free from all innovations throughout history.
Correction: While Ibn Asakir claims the Maliki school repudiates major innovations like Mu'tazila and anthropomorphism, he acknowledges that some Malikis later adopted Sufi extremism from Ibn Tumart's time.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Asakir identify as the reason for the Maliki school's freedom from Mu'tazila and anthropomorphism?
A) Malik's prohibition of narrating hadiths about God's attributes
B) The strict adherence to Ash'ari theology
C) The political support of the Abbasid caliphs
D) The influence of Sufi masters

Key Takeaway
The Maliki school's purity from major innovations is attributed to Imam Malik's proactive measure of prohibiting the narration of attribute hadiths, though later Sufi extremism appeared.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever uncritically accepted a belief about God's attributes that could lead to anthropomorphism? How does Malik's precautionary measure challenge you to examine the sources of your beliefs about Allah? What concrete step will you take to ensure your understanding of Allah's attributes remains within the bounds of the Salaf?
?
If you follow a different school, how do you guard against innovations in your school's approach to hadiths? What can you learn from Malik's proactive prohibition to protect your own faith from subtle deviations? Will you commit to studying the position of your imams on such narrations?
?
The passage warns of later Sufi extremism even within the Maliki school. In your spiritual journey, are there areas where you might be leaning towards excess? How can you balance devotion with adherence to the Sunnah, and what will you change starting today?

The Hanbali School: From Hashwiyya to Orthodoxy
Ibn Asakir outlines the diverse factions within the Hanbali school and its eventual alignment with Sunni orthodoxy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical evolution of the Hanbali school from predominantly Hashwiyya to mainstream Sunnism through interaction with Sunni scholars.

Core Idea
Most Hanbalis were originally Hashwiyya but were corrected through engagement with Sunni scholars.

Ibn Asakir continues his analysis by examining the Hanbali school. He notes that while some Hanbalis followed the salaf in tafwid (consigning ambiguous verses to God) and some inclined towards Mu'tazila, the majority over the centuries adhered to Hashwiyya thought, following the Salimiyya and Karramiyya methods. The turning point came when Caliph al-Zahir established judgeships for all four Sunni schools, leading Hanbalis to connect with Sunni scholars and gradually shed their innovated beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Hanbalis
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three categories of Hanbalis mentioned: salafi tafwid, Mu'tazila, and Hashwiyya.
The Hashwiyya are the majority.
2
Identify the transformation
What event caused the Hanbalis to change? How did it happen?
Al-Zahir's appointment of judges for four schools led to scholarly interaction.
3
Trace the evolution
The passage describes a progression: from being mostly Hashwiyya to connecting with Sunni scholars and then their 'innovated diseases' disappearing.
This is a narrative of purification through engagement.
4
Reflect on diversity within schools
Note that the Hanbali school was not monolithic; it contained various subgroups.

The Hashwi Figure from Harran and His Downfall
Ibn Asakir narrates the story of a Hashwi figure from Harran who schemed to replace the Ash'ari school with Hashwiyya and was refuted by scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Hashwi figure from Harran failed in his attempt to replace the Ash'ari school with Hashwiyya, and how this illustrates the strength of Sunni theological scholarship.

Core Idea
Without proper training, even the most eloquent preacher cannot challenge established Sunni theology.

Ibn Asakir presents a historical example of a man who, despite intelligence and eloquence, attempted to undermine the Ash'ari school. His story serves as a cautionary tale about the necessity of a solid scholarly foundation. In this lesson, we examine how his lack of training in theoretical sciences led to his downfall, according to Ibn Asakir.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the full narrative.
Notice the key terms: جالية حران, حشوية, أشاعرة, etc.
2
Identify the reasons for failure
As you read, pinpoint the specific reasons Ibn Asakir gives for the figure's failure. What does he say about his knowledge and training?
Look for the phrase about lacking a teacher in theoretical sciences.
3
Reflect on the lesson's message
Consider how this story reinforces the importance of scholarly training and the resilience of Sunni theology.
Why does Ibn Asakir emphasize the 'strength of arguments' of the Ash'aris and Maturidis?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لولا جالية حران بعد نكبة بغداد حطوا رحلهم بالشام ونبغ من بينهم رجل حسنت نشأته في الطلب على ذكاء وحافظة و سمت وتمكن من اجتلاب ثقة شيوخ العلم الى نفسه وثنائهم عليه وكان واعظاً طلق اللسان فاذا هو يجرى على خطة مدبرة في احلال المذهب الحشوى تحت ستار مذهب السف[؟] محل مذهب أهل السنة ولم يعلم أن مذهب أهل السنة من الأشاعرة والماتريدية بلغ من التمحيص العلمي على تعاقب القرون بأيدى نوابغ اهل النظر والفقه فى الدين ممن لا يعد هذا الحشوي من صغار تلامذتهم الى مستوى من قوة الحجج بحيث إذا حاول مثله ان يصطدم بها لا يقع الا علي أم رأسه فيردى ولا يودى وحيث لم يكن له شيخ يرشده في العلوم النظرية اصبح علمه لا يرتكن على شئ وثيق خليطا كثير التناقض توزعت مواهبه في الهواء متعبة ثم افضى الى ما عمل وزالت فتنه برد العلماء عليه
Had it not been for the community of Harran after the calamity of Baghdad, who set down their baggage in Syria, there emerged among them a man whose upbringing in seeking knowledge was good, with intelligence, memory, and high aspiration, and he was able to attract the trust of the scholars to himself and their praise of him, and he was an eloquent preacher. Then he began to follow a premeditated plan to replace the Ash'ari school with the Hashwi school under the guise of the school of al-Suf [?], instead of the school of Ahl al-Sunna. He did not know that the school of Ahl al-Sunna from the Ash'aris and Maturidis had reached, through the ages, a level of scientific scrutiny by the geniuses of theoretical scrutiny and religious jurisprudence, such that this Hashwi is not even counted among their junior students, a level of strength of arguments such that if someone like him tries to collide with them, he falls on his head and is destroyed but does not destroy. And since he did not have a teacher to guide him in theoretical sciences, his knowledge became not based on anything solid, mixed with much contradiction, his talents scattered in the air, exhausting. Then he proceeded to what he did, and his sedition ceased by the refutation of scholars upon him.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage narrates the story of a specific Hashwi figure who emerged from the Harran community after the sack of Baghdad. He was intelligent and eloquent, and he gained the trust of scholars. He schemed to replace the Ash'ari school (the school of Ahl al-Sunna) with the Hashwi school under the guise of another school (possibly 'al-Suf' or 'al-Salaf'). The author emphasizes that the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools had been refined over centuries by great scholars, and this man was not even a minor student among them. When he tried to challenge them, he was utterly defeated and his fitna ended by the refutation of scholars. The author points out that he lacked a teacher in theoretical sciences, so his knowledge was unsound, contradictory, and scattered. Thus, the lesson illustrates the strength of Sunni scholarship against incompetent challengers.

حشوية
أشاعرة
ماتريدية
فتنة
علوم نظرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any charismatic preacher can successfully challenge established Sunni theology.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that without proper scholarly training in theoretical sciences, one's knowledge is unsound and contradictory, leading to inevitable failure against the well-reasoned arguments of the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, why did the Hashwi figure from Harran fail?
A) The caliph arrested him.
B) He lacked a teacher in theoretical sciences and his knowledge was unsound.
C) He was not intelligent enough.
D) He was rejected by the Harran community.

Key Takeaway
The story of the Hashwi figure illustrates that Sunni theological scholarship, refined over centuries, is impervious to unqualified challengers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The man had intelligence, memory, and the trust of scholars, yet he led people astray. What in your own Islamic knowledge acquisition might rely on similar superficial strengths—cleverness, ability to memorize, or the approval of others—without the solid foundation of a teacher and rigorous study? What would it cost you to continue on that path?
?
This passage says the Sunni school's arguments are so strong that anyone who collides with them is destroyed. But perhaps you secretly doubt whether your own convictions could withstand a challenge. What specific area of your aqeedah are you most hesitant to examine closely, and what would it cost you to study that area with a qualified teacher?

Political Origins of the Khawarij and Shi'a
Ibn Asakir identifies political passion and heretical infiltration as origins of Khawarij and Shi'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Asakir considers the Khawarij and Shi'a to have political rather than religious origins.

Core Idea
Political passion, not knowledge, gave birth to the Khawarij and Shi'a.

This lesson explores the origins of two major sects. Ibn Asakir argues that they were not the product of genuine theological inquiry but of political fervor, later exploited by heretics. Understanding this helps us see the author's historical approach to sectarianism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence once, then the translation. Notice the author's strong opening statement.
2
Identify the two causes
Find where the author mentions political passion and infiltration by heretics. These are the key factors.
3
Observe the direction of these sects
The author states their original direction is hostility towards the established government. Highlight this phrase.
4
Reflect on the author's tone
Consider why the author emphasizes that knowledge had no role. This is a strong dismissal of their religious legitimacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجلى أنه لا دخل للعلم فى نشأة الخوارج والشيعة بل و لدتهما العاطفة السياسية ثم اندس فيهما خصوم الدين من الزنادقة فتطورتا أطواراً شائنة واتجاههما الأصلى نحو خصومة الحكومة القائمة، والمرجئة وليدة نوع من البحث العلمي اتجاهها نحو معاكسة الخوارج في المعتقد ثم تشعبت منها آراء بعيدة عن الدين والعلم أورثت التهاون فى العمل. والجبرية دعاة الخمود ونذير الدمار نتجت عن بحث غیر علمی علوقها من مجاورة السمنية والبراهمة وغيرهما من فرق الاباحة والخمول . والقدرية نشأت من بحث علمى ووجهتها نحو خصومه الكسل والتواكل وباعتبار ما تطور اليها متأثرة ببعض آراء الثنوية . والحشوية أسقطها الجهل والجمود ترتئى آراء جاهلية ورثتها من نحل كانوا عليها قبل الإسلام وراحت عليهم تمويهات المموهين من الثنوية وأهل الكتاب والصائبة، لهم تقشف يخدعون به العامة وجهالات لا يتصورها عاقل وهو غلاظ الطباع قساة جنماة يتحينون الفرص لاحداث القلاقل لا يظهر لهم قول الا عند ضعف الاسلام ويستفحل أمر الالحاد مع ظهور قولهم هكذا في جميع ادوار التاريخ، خصومتهم متوجهة نحو العقل والعلوم النظرية وكل فرقة قائمة.
And it is clear that knowledge had no role in the emergence of the Khawarij and the Shi'a; rather, political passion gave birth to them, then enemies of the religion from among the heretics infiltrated them, and they developed into shameful stages, and their original direction was towards hostility against the established government. The Murji'a is the offspring of a kind of scholarly research, its direction towards opposing the Khawarij in belief, then branches of opinions far from religion and knowledge sprang from it, which caused negligence in action. The Jabriyya are preachers of lethargy and heralds of destruction, arising from unscientific research, hanging from proximity to the Samaniyya and Brahmins and other groups of licentiousness and inactivity. The Qadariyya arose from scholarly research, and its direction was towards opposing laziness and fatalism, but taking into account what it developed into, influenced by some dualist ideas. The Hashwiyya were brought down by ignorance and rigidity; they hold pre-Islamic opinions that they inherited from the religions they had before Islam, and the deceptions of the deceivers from the dualists, People of the Book, and Sabians were effective on them. They have asceticism by which they deceive the commoners, and ignorant notions that no intelligent person can conceive, and they are coarse-natured, cruel, bloodthirsty, awaiting opportunities to cause turmoil. Their opinion only appears when Islam is weak, and the matter of atheism becomes rampant with the emergence of their opinion, thus in all periods of history. Their hostility is directed towards reason and theoretical sciences, and every sect exists [in this way].
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage classifies several sects according to their origins. The author begins with the Khawarij and Shi'a, stating that they were not born from knowledge but from political passion, and then heretics infiltrated them. Their primary drive is hostility to the government. The Murji'a, in contrast, arose from theological research aimed at opposing the Khawarij, but eventually produced opinions far from religion, leading to laxity in action. The Jabriyya (fatalists) arose from unscientific research, influenced by contact with Buddhist-like groups (Samaniyya) and Brahmins. The Qadariyya (free-will advocates) arose from scholarly research aimed at opposing fatalism, but later became influenced by dualist (Manichaean) ideas. Finally, the author gives an extended critique of the Hashwiyya: they are characterized by ignorance and rigidity, holding pre-Islamic beliefs, easily deceived by dualists and others. They appear only when Islam is weak, and they are hostile to reason and theoretical sciences. This lesson shows the author's historical and genealogical approach to understanding sects.

Key Terms
خوارج
شيعة
زنادقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Khawarij and Shi'a arose purely from theological disputes.
Correction: Ibn Asakir emphasizes their political roots, stating that knowledge had no role.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Asakir, what gave birth to the Khawarij and Shi'a?
A) Scholarly research
B) Political passion
C) Infiltration by heretics
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij and Shi'a originated from political passion, not religious knowledge, and their core orientation is opposition to government.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a political or social opinion you hold strongly. Did it come from Islamic knowledge or from passion? How can you ensure your views are rooted in knowledge before acting on them?
?
The passage says heretics infiltrated these groups when passion overshadowed knowledge. In your own life, where might your passions be making you vulnerable to misguided influences? What steps can you take to protect your faith?
?
The Khawarij and Shi'a directed hostility towards the established government. Examine your attitude towards those in authority over you—your parents, teachers, or leaders. Is your stance based on knowledge and justice, or on mere emotion? Commit to one concrete change.

Scholarly Origins of the Murji'a and Qadariyya
Ibn Asakir describes the Murji'a and Qadariyya as products of scholarly research, then shows their corruptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Murji'a and Qadariyya emerged from theological research but deviated into extremes.

Core Idea
The Murji'a and Qadariyya began as scholarly responses but later absorbed foreign influences.

Unlike the previous sects, the Murji'a and Qadariyya originated from intellectual efforts. However, the author shows that they eventually strayed from true religion. This lesson unpacks their origins and how they developed.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation for both sects
Read the two sentences together. They are parallel: each sect is introduced with its origin and then its deviation.
2
Note the shared origin
Both are described as arising from 'bahth 'ilmi' (scholarly research). Underline this phrase.
3
Identify their initial directions
Murji'a opposed Khawarij; Qadariyya opposed laziness and fatalism. This shows they started as correctives.
4
Observe how each deviated
Murji'a led to negligence in action; Qadariyya absorbed dualist ideas. The author critiques both outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجلى أنه لا دخل للعلم فى نشأة الخوارج والشيعة بل و لدتهما العاطفة السياسية ثم اندس فيهما خصوم الدين من الزنادقة فتطورتا أطواراً شائنة واتجاههما الأصلى نحو خصومة الحكومة القائمة، والمرجئة وليدة نوع من البحث العلمي اتجاهها نحو معاكسة الخوارج في المعتقد ثم تشعبت منها آراء بعيدة عن الدين والعلم أورثت التهاون فى العمل. والجبرية دعاة الخمود ونذير الدمار نتجت عن بحث غیر علمی علوقها من مجاورة السمنية والبراهمة وغيرهما من فرق الاباحة والخمول . والقدرية نشأت من بحث علمى ووجهتها نحو خصومه الكسل والتواكل وباعتبار ما تطور اليها متأثرة ببعض آراء الثنوية . والحشوية أسقطها الجهل والجمود ترتئى آراء جاهلية ورثتها من نحل كانوا عليها قبل الإسلام وراحت عليهم تمويهات المموهين من الثنوية وأهل الكتاب والصائبة، لهم تقشف يخدعون به العامة وجهالات لا يتصورها عاقل وهو غلاظ الطباع قساة جنماة يتحينون الفرص لاحداث القلاقل لا يظهر لهم قول الا عند ضعف الاسلام ويستفحل أمر الالحاد مع ظهور قولهم هكذا في جميع ادوار التاريخ، خصومتهم متوجهة نحو العقل والعلوم النظرية وكل فرقة قائمة.
And it is clear that knowledge had no role in the emergence of the Khawarij and the Shi'a; rather, political passion gave birth to them, then enemies of the religion from among the heretics infiltrated them, and they developed into shameful stages, and their original direction was towards hostility against the established government. The Murji'a is the offspring of a kind of scholarly research, its direction towards opposing the Khawarij in belief, then branches of opinions far from religion and knowledge sprang from it, which caused negligence in action. The Jabriyya are preachers of lethargy and heralds of destruction, arising from unscientific research, hanging from proximity to the Samaniyya and Brahmins and other groups of licentiousness and inactivity. The Qadariyya arose from scholarly research, and its direction was towards opposing laziness and fatalism, but taking into account what it developed into, influenced by some dualist ideas. The Hashwiyya were brought down by ignorance and rigidity; they hold pre-Islamic opinions that they inherited from the religions they had before Islam, and the deceptions of the deceivers from the dualists, People of the Book, and Sabians were effective on them. They have asceticism by which they deceive the commoners, and ignorant notions that no intelligent person can conceive, and they are coarse-natured, cruel, bloodthirsty, awaiting opportunities to cause turmoil. Their opinion only appears when Islam is weak, and the matter of atheism becomes rampant with the emergence of their opinion, thus in all periods of history. Their hostility is directed towards reason and theoretical sciences, and every sect exists [in this way].
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage classifies several sects according to their origins. The author begins with the Khawarij and Shi'a, stating that they were not born from knowledge but from political passion, and then heretics infiltrated them. Their primary drive is hostility to the government. The Murji'a, in contrast, arose from theological research aimed at opposing the Khawarij, but eventually produced opinions far from religion, leading to laxity in action. The Jabriyya (fatalists) arose from unscientific research, influenced by contact with Buddhist-like groups (Samaniyya) and Brahmins. The Qadariyya (free-will advocates) arose from scholarly research aimed at opposing fatalism, but later became influenced by dualist (Manichaean) ideas. Finally, the author gives an extended critique of the Hashwiyya: they are characterized by ignorance and rigidity, holding pre-Islamic beliefs, easily deceived by dualists and others. They appear only when Islam is weak, and they are hostile to reason and theoretical sciences. This lesson shows the author's historical and genealogical approach to understanding sects.

Key Terms
مرجئة
قدرية
ثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any sect that uses reason is acceptable.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that even scholarly origins can lead to corruption, as with the Murji'a and Qadariyya.

Checkpoint
What was the initial direction of the Qadariyya?
A) To oppose the Khawarij
B) To oppose laziness and fatalism
C) To promote free will
D) To support the government

Key Takeaway
Both the Murji'a and Qadariyya began as scholarly movements but deviated due to extremism and foreign influences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a matter of religion, do you actively guard against allowing your research to drift away from the Quran and Sunnah? How can you ensure that your pursuit of knowledge does not lead to neglect of actions, as happened with the Murji'a?
?
Have you ever found yourself leaning toward a position simply to oppose another group, without grounding it in revelation? What safeguards do you have against this error?
?
The Qadariyya started by opposing fatalism but ended up absorbing foreign dualist ideas. What foreign ideas or cultural assumptions might unconsciously shape your understanding of Islam today? Will you examine your beliefs to purge any such influence?

Jabriyya: Unscientific Fatalism
Ibn Asakir judges the Jabriyya as unscientific and influenced by foreign fatalistic groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's view that the Jabriyya arose from unscientific methods and external influences.

Core Idea
The Jabriyya emerged not from science but from proximity to non-Islamic fatalistic groups.

The Jabriyya (fatalists) are presented as a sect born from 'unscientific research' and influenced by contact with Buddhist-like Samaniyya and Brahmins. This lesson unpacks that origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic sentence about the Jabriyya
Read the short sentence carefully. It packs multiple criticisms.
2
Identify the key descriptors
Find 'دعاة الخمود' (preachers of lethargy), 'نذير الدمار' (heralds of destruction), and 'بحث غير علمي' (unscientific research).
3
Note the external influence
The author says their origin is from proximity (مجاورة) to Samaniyya and Brahmins. Underline these groups.
4
Reflect on the author's language
The harsh terms show the author's strong rejection of Jabriyya as a deviant sect.

Source Text

The Jabriyya are preachers of lethargy and heralds of destruction, arising from unscientific research, hanging from proximity to the Samaniyya and Brahmins and other groups of licentiousness and inactivity. The Qadariyya arose from scholarly research, and its direction was towards opposing laziness and fatalism, but taking into account what it developed into, influenced by some dualist ideas. They have asceticism by which they deceive the commoners, and ignorant notions that no intelligent person can conceive, and they are coarse-natured, cruel, bloodthirsty, awaiting opportunities to cause turmoil. Hashwiyya: Ignorance and Hostility to Reason
Ibn Asakir condemns the Hashwiyya as ignorant, rigid, and hostile to reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Asakir's comprehensive critique of the Hashwiyya as ignorant, rigid, and hostile to reason.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya are characterized by ignorance, pre-Islamic ideas, and an aggressive stance against rational inquiry.

The Hashwiyya receive the longest treatment. The author paints them as products of ignorance and rigidity, holding onto pre-Islamic beliefs and easily deceived. They are described as coarse, violent, and opportunistic, appearing only when Islam is weak. This lesson explores that characterization.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire Arabic description of the Hashwiyya
This is a long, dense sentence. Read it slowly, then the translation.
2
List the negative attributes
Identify all the negative qualities: ignorance, pre-Islamic opinions, asceticism for deception, coarse nature, cruelty, opportunism, hostility to reason.
3
Find the condition of their appearance
The author says they only appear when Islam is weak. Highlight this condition.
4
Observe the final target
Their hostility is directed towards reason and theoretical sciences. This is a key point.

Source Text

The Mu'tazila: Scholarly Origins and Praiseworthy Contributions
Ibn Asakir acknowledges the Mu'tazila's scholarly origins and their role in defending Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Asakir highlights the Mu'tazila's scholarly roots, their rational defense of Islam, and the valuable works of scholars like al-Jahiz and Qadi Abd al-Jabbar.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila were scholarly defenders of Islam, not merely heretics.

Ibn Asakir begins this passage by contrasting the Mu'tazila with the Hashwiyya, emphasizing their intellectual origins. He then details their project of defending Islam against external ideologies using reason, and praises specific scholars and books. This lesson explores the positive side of the Mu'tazila that is often overlooked.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening contrast
Notice how Ibn Asakir positions the Mu'tazila as the opposite of the Hashwiyya, born from scholarly research.
The Hashwiyya were known for literalist and anthropomorphic tendencies.
2
Identify their project
The Mu'tazila aimed to repel external ideas using conclusive proofs. List the groups they defended against.
3
Examine the praise for specific works
Read the praise for al-Jahiz's book on prophethood and Qadi Abd al-Jabbar's Tathbit. What does this tell us about the author's view?
Al-Dhahabi's mercy on al-Jahiz is a significant endorsement.
4
Reflect on the teacher's practice
Ibn Asakir mentions his own teacher using Mu'tazili arguments. This shows their arguments were valued.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة على ضد الحشوية بخط مستقيم أنتجها البحث العلمي، ساقهم شره عقولهم إلى محاولة اكتناه كل شيء وعداؤهم الأصلي نحو الجمود وخطتهم دفع الآراء المتسربة من الخارج إلى الإسلام بحجج دامغة وأدلة عقلية مفحمة ولهم مواقف شريفة في الدفاع عن الدين الإسلامي إزاء الدهريين ومنكرى النبوة والثنوية والنصارى واليهود والصابئة وأصناف الملاحدة، وترى الذهبي يترحم على الجاحظ في سير النبلاء حين يذكر كتابه في النبوة، ولم نر مايقارب کتاب تثبيت دلائل النبوة للقاضي عبد الجبار (۱) في قوة الحجاج وحسن الصياغة فى دفع شكوك المشككين وليس بجيد الأعراض الكلى عن كتبهم وكم فيها من الفوائد التي لاتزال في أثوابها القشيبة لم تبل بكرور الزمن عليها . وكم كان الاستاذ الإمام يجاد فيها ما يدفع به خصوم العصر ولا يتحاشى عن الأخذ به من غير بخس . لحقهم إلا أنهم لكثرة استغالهم بمناظرة الاخصام عدت منهم إلى عقولهم آراء ابتعدوا بها عن الصواب وانغمسوا في بدع ردها الأصحاب، قال الخطابي صاحب معالم السنن : كانت المعتزلة فى الزمان الأول على خلاف هذه الأهواء وإنما أحدثها بعضهم في الزمان المتأخر.
And the Mu'tazila are the direct opposite of the Hashwiyya, produced by scholarly research. Their greed for [the use of] their minds drove them to attempt to comprehend everything, and their original enmity was towards rigidity. Their plan was to repel the ideas that seeped into Islam from outside with conclusive proofs and overwhelming rational evidence. They have honorable stances in defending the Islamic religion against the materialists (dahriyyun), deniers of prophethood, dualists, Christians, Jews, Sabians, and all kinds of atheists. You see al-Dhahabi praying for mercy for al-Jahiz in Siyar al-Nubala' when he mentions his book on prophethood. And we have not seen anything approaching the book 'Tathbit Dala'il al-Nubuwwa' of Qadi Abd al-Jabbar in strength of argument and good composition in repelling the doubts of skeptics. And it is not good to completely turn away from their books; how many benefits are in them that still remain in their fresh garments, not worn out by the passage of time. And how often did the teacher imam (likely Ibn Asakir's teacher) debate with them, taking what repels the opponents of the age, not refraining from taking it without detracting. They are to be followed [in that], except that due to their excessive occupation with debating opponents, ideas entered their minds that strayed from correctness, and they immersed themselves in innovations that the scholars rejected. Al-Khattabi, author of Ma'alim al-Sunan, said: 'The Mu'tazila in the early period were different from these inclinations; only some of them later invented them in the later period.'
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents a nuanced evaluation of the Mu'tazila. The author begins by stating that the Mu'tazila are the direct opposite of the Hashwiyya, born from scholarly research. He acknowledges their intellectual drive to understand everything and their initial opposition to rigidity. Their project was to defend Islam from external ideas using rational arguments. He praises their efforts against various opponents, and even notes that al-Dhahabi sympathized with al-Jahiz's book on prophethood. He highly praises the book 'Tathbit Dala'il al-Nubuwwa' by Qadi Abd al-Jabbar for its strength and composition. He cautions against completely discarding their works, as they contain lasting benefits. He mentions that his own teacher would use their arguments to refute contemporary opponents without hesitation. However, he points out that their excessive engagement with opponents led to some of them adopting erroneous ideas and innovations, which later scholars rejected. He then quotes al-Khattabi to reinforce that the early Mu'tazila were not like the later ones who introduced innovations. Thus, the author gives credit where due but also marks the boundary of their deviation.

Key Terms
معتزلة
جاحظ
قاضي عبد الجبار
تثبيت دلائل النبوة
ذهبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila have no value and their books should be completely rejected.
Correction: Ibn Asakir shows that their works contained valuable arguments and were used by scholars.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Asakir say about the book 'Tathbit Dala'il al-Nubuwwa' by Qadi Abd al-Jabbar?
A) It is weak in argument.
B) It is unmatched in strength and composition.
C) It is full of errors.
D) It should be burned.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila, despite later innovations, produced valuable scholarly works in defense of Islam that even orthodox scholars acknowledged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author's teacher used Mu'tazili arguments to refute opponents. Are you willing to learn from someone you fundamentally disagree with in order to defend the truth? What prevents you from doing so?
?
This passage acknowledges that the Mu'tazila, despite their errors, produced valuable works. Have you ever dismissed an entire group's contributions because of their mistakes? How can you adopt a more balanced approach in evaluating those you disagree with?
?
Reflect on your own attitude towards scholars or groups you consider misguided. Are you quick to discard their insights entirely? What would change if you followed the example of al-Dhahabi and Ibn Asakir in recognizing beneficial aspects even from those with whom you disagree?
